THE RED PAGE .
Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A.s full as the days are of light in midsummer his
life was of Love.
Oh, the year on the mountain, the year by the
river, the year on the plain !
As full as the nights are of light when the rounded
white moon sails above,
Was his life of the Love that went from him to
never come to him again !
The mountain at sunrise rose out of the darkness
to welcome the light,
With a glitter of silvery eyes in its faoets and
fissures of stone,
And op ning of buds on the bushes and spreading
of petals of white—
But his welcome of Love was prostration of
heart on the steps of a throne!
The year by the river, slow-flowing, slow-flowing;
the year on the plain ;
Oh, the day-skies and night-skies were eostaoies
rolling round spirit-fed sight 1
But then the two lives fell asunder; and whirl-
winds, splashed with chill rain,
Cried above the brute chaos : “ Night, endlessly!
moonleBsly, starlessly Night 1”
Sydney.
PI.
In the Clouds of Fantasy.
In reality, men and women are nothing
but balloons : they all have a globe of spiri
and a car of flesh. Sometimes—most times
indeed—the globe never gets inflated, anc
the car remains on earth— fulfilling ever s<
many useful purposes and ever so few tha
are beautiful. And sometimes a man has
just spirit enough to make him a captive
balloon—he would like to soar higher if he
could ; but his fleshy car is too heavy—he is
tethered always to the ground. And rarely yoi
find an individual—like Blake, or Shelley, or
Keats—whose head is pure hydrogen, anc
who ca rries so little mat erial ballast that he has
hardly touched earth before he is away again
for ever, a speck in the ether almost undis-
cemible by the grosser souls he leaves behind
him. Only sometimes he keeps on singing,
singing, singing as lie mounts, and to those
who would fain follow, but who are left be-
hind like the little lame boy of Hamelin, the
mere song of what he sees is very precious.
• • * *
Richard Le Gallienne is maybe but a cap-
tive balloon, yet his rope of fancy is long
enough to let him reach the clouds, and
sometimes for a moment, one thinks, he
must have escaped from earth wholly. His
“Prose Fancies ” (there are two series of
them now) are better than his poems;
he is as puss- dainty in them, and
freer; and when his mood is at its rarest he
sways you without labor. And for criticism
there needs only quotation.
Science says that the moon is a dead world, a
cosmic ship littered with the skeletons of its crew,
and from which every rat of vitality has lon* since
escaped. It is the ghost that rises from its tomb
every night, to haunt its faithless lover, the
world. It is a country of ancient silver-mines,
unworked fur centuries. You may seethe gaping
mouths of the dark old shafts through your tele-
scope. You may even see the rusting pit-tackle,
the ruinous engine-houses, and the idle pick and
shovel. Or you may say that it is counterfeit
silver, coined to take in the young fools who love
to gaze upon it. It is, so to speak, a bad half-
crown.
As you will! but lam of Endymion’s belief—
and no one was ever more intimate with the
moon. For me the moon is a country of great
seaports, whither all the ships of our dreams come
home. From all quarters of the world, every day
of the week, there are ships sailing to the moon.
They are the ships that sail just when and where
you please. You take your passage on that con-
dition. And it is ridiculous to think for what a
trifle the oaptain will take you on so long a jour-
ney. If you want to come baok, just to take an
excursion and no more, just to take a lighted look
at those coasts of rose and pearl, he will ask no
more than a glass or two of bright wine—indeed
when the captain is very kind, a flower will take
you there and back in no time; if you want to
stay whole days there, but still come back dreamy
and strange, you may take a little dark root and
smoke it in a silver pipe, or you may drink a little
pfnal of poppy-juioe. and thus you shall find the
Land of Heart s Desire ; but if you are wise and
would stay in that land for ever, the terms are
even easier— a little powder into a phial of wate
a little piece of lead no bigger than a pea, and
farthing s-worth of explosive fire, and thus als
you are in the Land of Heart’s Desire for ever.
I dreamed last night that I stood on thebluste
ing windy wharf, and the dark ship was there
it was impatient, like all of us, to leave th
world. Its funnels belched black smoke, its engine
throbbed against the quay like arms that wer
eager to strike and be done, and a bell was beatin
impatient summons to be gone. The dark captai
stood ready on the bridge, and he looked into eac
of our faces as we passed on board. ‘ls it for th
long voyage ? ’ he said. ‘ Yes ! the lon- voyage
1 said—and his stern eyes seemed to soften as
answered.
At last we were all aboard, and in the twinkhn
of an eye were out of sight of land. Yet, one
afloat, it seemed as though we should never reac
our port in the moon—so it seemed to me as I la l
awake in my little cabin, listening to the patien
thud and throb of the great screws, beating in th
ships side like a human heart. . . .
There were three friends I had especially se
out to see ; two young lovers who had emigratec
to those colonies in the moon just after their mar
riage, and there was another. What a surpris
it would be to all three, for I had written no lette
to say I was coming. Indeed it was just a sudden
impulse, the pistol-flash of a long desire.
. I tried to imagine what the town would be like
in which they were now living. I asked the
captain, and he answered with a sad smile that i
would be just exactly as I cared to dream it.
Oh, well then,’ I thought, ‘ I know what i
Will be like. There shall be a great restless, toss
ing estuary, with Atlantic winds for ever ruffling
the sails of busy ships, ships coming home with
laughter, ships leaving home with sad sea-gul
°u le l h ,,rew eh. And the shaggy, tossing water
shall be bounded on either bank with high grand
walls, and on one bank shall be a fretted spire
soaring with a jangle of bells, from amid a tangle
of masts, and underneath the bells and the masts
shall go streets rising up from the strand, streets
full of faces, and sweet with the smell of tar and
the sea. O captain ! will it be morning or night
when we come to my city ? In the morning my
city is like a sea-blown rose, in the night it is
bright as the sailor’s star.
‘lf it be early morning; what shall I do? I
shall run to the house in which my friends lie in
happy sleep, never to be parted again, and kiss
my hand to their shrouded window, and then I
shall run on and on till the city is behind and the
sweetness of country lanes is about me, and I
shall gather flowers as I run, from sheer wanton-
ness ; and then at last, flushed and breathless,
I shall stand beneath her window, I shall stand
and listen, and I shall hear her breathing right
through the heavy curtains, and the hushed garden
and sleeping house will bid me keep silence, but I
shall cry a great cry up to the morning star, and
say, “No, I will not keep silence. Mine is the
voice she listens for in her sleep. She will wake
again for no voice but mine. Dear one, awake;
the.morning of all mornings has ooine
As I write, the moon looks down at me like a
Madonna from the great canvas of the sky. She
seems beautiful with the beauty of all the eyes
that have looked up at her, sad with all the tears
of all those eyes ; like a silver bowl brimming with
the tears of dead lovers she seems. Yes, there are
seaports in the moon; there are ships to take us
there.
• * * *
Comment on this anti-climaxes as abomin-
ably as the parson’s announcement of next
Sunday s collection before the organ-echoes
have done ringing through the aisles ; but it
should be said that one personal reference is
to Le Gallienne’s young wife (he himself is
only 31) whom he lost not long ago after a
year of idyllic marriage. (“They learn in
suffering what they teach in song”—and
coin their griefs to drachmas at the pub-
lisher’s.) And to close with a curious proof
of the human mind’s antipodes : compare the
beautiful ending above with another ending
also previously quoted on this page, but not
in juxtaposition—the reflection which con-
cludes Frank Swettenham’s description of a
Malayan moonrise :
1 here is nothing cold about this Eastern moon.
Seen, half-risen, against the dark foliage of the
mountain, it glitters like molten silver, dazzling
the eyes, and as it soars serenely upward seems
the very perfection of beauty, light, and purity.
Strange that the delight and glory of mankind
since ever the earth was peopled, the emblem of
unattainable longing, should be only a gigantic
cinder.
Notes.
William Main’s “ Bush Solitudes and
Other Verses ” (Geo. Robertson) are homely old-
nian’s-rhymes, lacking intellectual value or in-
terest, but occasionally getting in a quaint heart-
appealing touch. And these rough lines stand
out sufficiently for quotation :
For a man that keeps a bush puh. can’t afford to have a
heai t ;
lie’s got to he a human parasile,
Manipulating poison, keeping men on till they part
Tueir last stiver—turn them out whatever night.
And if there’s any trouble, why, the constable’s at hand
It need not trouble him, for that’s the way
To carry on the business, in a free, enlightened land—
Kind hearts and decent grog don’t seem to pay.
Why, by-the-by, do so many old men turn t
rhyming? Is it a symptom of senile degeneration
or an outlet for no-longer-head-controlled emo
tions ? or a kill-time when the poor back-numbe
sits lonely in the chimney-corner unheeded by
Voutii hurrying past in a turmoil of alien thought
and aspirations ? And you may note, too, tha ;
old Scotchmen are particularly venomous : the
Irish mostly rhyme when they’re young: the Eng
lish don’t rhyme—-because they eat too much, and
keep their blood in their stomachs?

An article on “ Kipling in India ” in M'Cluhe’s
for July is worth looking at. An Indian office
wrote to a friend in Australia a while ago :
“You ask what we think of Kipling out here
Personally I can never dissociate him from his
odious personality—a little, ugly, spectacled, sen
suous cad ; absolutely lacking in manly qualities.
Still, there is no doubt that he is a perfect genius
in his way ; his marvellous powers of observation
and his knack of picking up the smallest details
deserve no other name. He is afraid of a horse,
and loathes the sight of one, yet he will write i
better account of a steeplechase than many a
racing man. He has seen a battery of artillery
once or twice, and perhaps talked occasionally to
gunners, and he can write pages of jargon which
no one outside the Royal Artillery can under-
stand. His writings are wonderfully true to life,
though, of course, a little exaggerated here and
there, but wonderfully little on the whole.’
A between.lines reading gets something of the
same result from E. Kay Robinson’s article.
Among Kipling’s former attributes are mentioned
his “stoop, heavy eyebrows, spectacles, sallow An-
glo-Indian complexion, jerky speech, abrupt move-
ments ... no sportsman . . . indifferent horse-
man ” — n °f> a probably-popular compound. But
compensations are noted : and it looks as if K.’s
(and others’) knowledge of his intellectual superi-
ority accounted for the unpopularity alleged
against him by our Australian’s correspondent.
Again, Kipling’s original sketches—or many of
them—are represented as being done after a hard
day’s office-grind ; and a man who stews his brain
for 10 or 12 hours a day is almost bound
to be moody and disliked. He has no vitality
to spare for social trifles. And Kipling
worked with all his heart and soul and strength
whence, according to a favorite theory of this
page, his success. Some wise women have an idea
that an easy baby is a poor baby ; and when you
look at the physiological chances there’s some-
thing in it. For, generally speaking, big heads
mean big brains, and big brains mean big men
wherefore, big men mean big pangs in parturition.
And isn’t there some analogy between childbirth
and bookbirth? So that—it is curious how all
tho thoughts of the world, like all its material
substances, spring from a handful of elements
perhaps (who knows?) from one element—you
come back to the maxims of much for much, little
for little, for everything payment. It is all poeti-
cally put in one of Olive .Schreiner’s “Dreams”
—which are not too well-known to debar quota-
tion :
THE ARTIST’S SECRET.
There was an artist, once, and he painted a picture.
Other artists had colors richer and rarer, and painted
more notable picture- He painted his with one color
there was a wonderiul red glow on it; and the people
went up and down, saying, •« We like the picture, we like
tile glow. ’
The other artists came and said, “ Where does he vet
Ins color from ? They asked him ; and he smiled and
said, ‘ I cannot tell you ;’’ and worked on with his head
bent low.
And one went to the far East and bought costlv pig.
ments, and made a rare color and painted, but after a
time the picture faded. Another read in the old books
and made a color rich and rare, but when he had nut it
on r.lie picture it was dead. r
Hut the artist painted on. Always the work got redder
and redder, and the artist grew whiter and whiter. At
last one day they found him dead before his picture and
they look him up to bury him. The other men looked
about m all the pots and crucibles, but they found noth-
ing they had not.
And when they undressed him to put his grave-olothes
on him, they found above his left breast the mark of a
wound—it was an oht, old wound, that, must have been
there all his life, for the edges were old and hardened :
but Death, who seals all things, had drawn the edges
together, and closed it up.
And they buried him. And still the people went about
sayinjj, “ Where did he find his color from? ”
And it came to pass that after a while the artist was
forgotten—but the work lived.
Picturesquely put, and calling up a host of kindred
ideas : the noxious pill-advt.’s text “For the blood
is the life ” ; vampires ; the o’d life-elixir hunter,
craving that essential ingredient, the blood of a
new-born babe (see him in Dumas) ; the blood-
sacrifice for the life of a savage chief ; that which
purchases the eternal life of religious sectaries;
and a hundred others. On what dim troglodytish
thinker did the germ-idea flash? lie would bo
worth knowing.
Reprints.
[For absolute melody—the sweet succession of syllables
—“A Fountain of Tears” has no superior in English.
“Annabel Lee” equals it—no more; and maybe O’S.
owes something to Poe's influence. O’Shaughnessy, in the
strife of bards, is fairly enough claimed as a Celt, and he
had the ear and the fancy which are chief concomitants
of del tic verse. He was born 1844, die 1881. His scope
was limiied, and his performance hardly reached hi*
early promise.]
A Fountain of Tears.
If you go over desert and mountain,
Far into the country of sorrow,
To-day and to-night and to-morrow,
And maybe for months and for years ;
You shall come, with a heart that is bursting
For trouble and toiling and thirsting,
You shall certainly come to the fountain
At length,— to the Fountain of Tears.
Very peaceful the place is, and solely
For piteous lamenting and sighing,
And those who come living or dying
Alike from their hopes and their fears ;
Full of cypress-like shadows the place is.
And statues that cover their faces :
But out of the gloom springs the holy
And beautiful Fountain of Tears.
And it flows and it flows with a motion
So gentle and lovely and listless,
And murmurs a tune so resistless
To him who hath suffered and hears—
You shall surely—without a word spoken,
Kneel down there and know your heart broken.
And yield to the long curb’d emotion
That day by the Fountain of Tears.
For it grows and it grows, as though leaping
Up higher the more one is thinking ;
And even its tunes go on sinking
More poignantly into the ears :
Yea, so blessed and good seems that fountain,
Reached after dry desert and mountain,
You shall fall down at length in your weeping
And bathe your sad face in the tears.
Then, alas ! while you lie there a season,
And sob between living and dying,
And give up the land you were trying
To find ’mid your hopes and your fears .
O the World shall come up and pass o’er you :
Strong men shall not stay to care for you,
Nor wonder indeed for what reason
Your way should seem harder than theirs.
But perhaps, while you lie, never lifting
1 our cheek from the wet leaves it presses,
Nor caring to raise your wet tresses
And look how the cold world appears,—
O perhaps the mere silences round you
All things in that place grief hath found you,
tea, e’en to the clouds o’er you drifting
May soothe you somewhat through your tears.
You may feel, when a falling leaf brushes
\ our face, as though some one had kissed you *
Or think at least some one who missed you
Hath sent you a thought,—if that cheers;
Or a bird’s little song faint and broken,
May pass for a tender word spoken
—Enough, while around you there rushes
That life-drowning torrent of tears.
And the tears shall flow faster and faster,
Brim over, and baffle resistance,
And roll down bleared roads to each distance
Of past desolation and years ;
Till tb*y cover the place of each sorrow,
And leave you no Past and no morrow :
lor what man is able to master
And stem the great Fountain of Tears ?
But the floods of the tears meet and gather;
The sound of them all grows like thunder':
—0 into what bosom, I wonder
Is poured the whole sorrow of years ?
For Eternity only seems keeping
Account of the great human weeping ;
May God then, the Maker and Father—
May He find a plaoe for the tears !
ARTHUR O'SHAUGHNESSY.

BEFORE YOU

When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
I>, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
Cfeorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE MORTGAGE.
“ We will pay the debt, husband,” she said,
And blushed at the name (they were newly wed),
“ We’ll fight the mortgage and beat it, wife.”
He smiled at the word that crowned his life.
“ Yes ! you and I, with love for a charm,
Will surely win back our hillside farm.”
They fought the mortgage from dewy morn
With their hoes among the tasseled corn,
And at noon from out the pungent smoke
Came crashing tree and the axe’s stroke ;
And deep in the night on ev’ry hand
Blazed their forest fires to clear the land;
Working along, with many a jest
At their savings in the old ship’s chest,
Where they hid away the interest.
But a mortgage grows when crops are dry,
And a mortgage lives when cattle die,
And it flourishes when the bitter gale
Cuts the blossomed bough with slanting hail;
And one bad season would do more harm
Than dozens good for their hillside farm.
So the mortgage ground them year by year,
Took the old mare’s foal and the milker’s steer,
Stole from the matron’s step its grace,
And scored its lines on her husband’s face.
The corn they grew and the wine they pressed
Were swallowed up by the interest.
Like that gruesome plant that only thrives
On flesh, it reached to the children’s lives,
And held them, too, in its cramping band
The powerless serfs of the mortgaged land.
It forced the girls to the milking stool
When other children were still at school,
And their only son went out afar
To northern scrubs where the gold-fields are ;
Bor he said, “ You have toiled and now are old,
And gold must ever be fought with gold;
Let me then start on the northern track,
I’ll bring the price of the mortgage back.”
From the farther ridge a glimpse they had
Of mettled horses and eager lad —
Saw saddle-hack jflunge and pack-horse sjiy,
When he waved his hat in a last good-bye.
He was gone. Aye, gone where lost men go :
They would see him never ; never know
If the ranges hid, or tawny flood
Had wrapped his bones in the mangrove mud.
The weary waiting : the wearing dread—
“ If I but knew that my son was dead ! ”
And, later, “ Oh, had he only died
Here in our home by his mother’s side ! ”
What need to follow the dreary tale
Through dragging years to the clearing sale ?
They fought their fight till the setting sun,
And when old age grasped them the mortgage won.
Q.
R.A.F.
THE DINGO OF BRIGALOW GAP.
For K.G-. or coronet, kingdom or crown,
The boys on Kalangada care not a rap ;
But the honor they ask for is galloping down
The red and white dingo of Brigalow Gap;
He has beaten us fairly at every exchange,
He is hard to keep up with and harder to track ;
He knows every stone on the Brigalow Range
The fastest and wildest and worst of the pack.
Good horses behind him with rowels we’ve raked :
On Bogan the bushcrows are feasting their fill,
And Footstep is foundered and Starlight is staked,
But the red and white dingo makes light of us
still ;
For him a fast gallop is nothing but fun—
Too cunning to poison, to wary to trap,
You can’t get the sight of a rifle or gun
On the red and white dingo of Brigalow Gap.
He has tasted the Lincolns and fancies the breed,
He has tried the Kalangada culls for a change,
And we know that he’ll never go short of a feed
As long as our wethers run under the range.
He’s the scourge of the country, the plague of the
spot,
The curse of the owner, the bane of the boss,
For he’s got to be reckoned with, like it or not,
When the latter is squaring his profit and loss.
On the walls of the stable are trophies galore,
The goal and the guerdon of many a ride ;
And a place is reserved at the top of the door
For the honor of holding his red and white
hide ;
But the days and the weeks they go merrily by,
The skins on the stable-wall flutter and flap,
they have plenty of time to get shrivelled and dry
While they wait for the dingo of Brigalow Gap.
There are yellows and brindles and crossbreds of
black,
The pride of the station, the talk of the town,
But we’d gladly give all to be out on his track,
With his stride getting short and the crows
coming down.
His life may be safe, but, believe me, for that
He hasn’t Kalangada kindness to thank :
We tracked him two days ago over the flat,
We heard him last night at the Marathon Tank,
And we saw him to-day as he skirted the brush
A mile from the corner of Halliday s fence.
We took to him then with a cavalry rush,
And charged the thick scrub with more spirit
than sense: .
For, whenever that Devil’s Limb leads us a dance,
We gallop to glory, whatever may hap ;
But down in the gully we got our last glance
At the red-and-white dingo of Brigalow Gap.
He has conquered us fairly, we’re bound to confess
(We claim to be sportsmen, and know when
we’re beat);
His triumph the greater, our credit the less
That we ride fairly well, and our horses are fleet.
Perhaps, when the days of the dingo are done,
And the race, barring him, is extinct in the land,
When cocky-selectors have swallowed the run,
And various fortunes have scattered our band ;
Perhaps, when with sorrow that scarce can be
He watches the last of his foemen depart;
When the sheep-walks are furrowed and planted
with corn, , . ,
And the want of a gallop is breaking his heart,
When his life is a burden bereft of its joys,
That old age embitters and sicknesses sap,
He’ll suffer the greybeards that once were ' the
boys ”
To catch him on foot in the Brigalow Gap.
W. H. OGILVIE.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 12, 1896.
The South Australian Widow.
A MEASURE now before S.A. Parliament for
readjusting certain of the relations between
the old man and the new woman promises to
raise much social and political dust before it
is finally disposed of. It was introduced by
King O Malley, the glorified American
bagman and M.P:, and its main provision is
that, when the old man dies, his widow shall
inherit one-tliird of his property, despite all
wills, testaments, and codicils to the coiir
trary, and as much more of it as she can get.
That is to say, she may still inherit all the
property, or three-fourths or half of it, or any
other proportion she can secure, provided it
be not less than one-third, but at least one-
third of her husband’s net belongings are-to
be hers unconditionally when, he shuffles off,
and no document which he can leave behind
will deprive her of it. This proposal sits
down hard on, several legal traditions of
great antiquity, and the lawyers of S.A.
Assembly—Premier Kingston included—are.
mostly hostile as a natural consequence. It
badly infringes the liberty of the male sub-
ject to dispose of his property as he pleases,
and, to a great extent, to dispose,of his wife
also. It prohibits him absolutely throwing
her out when he is done with her. It also
prohibits him condemning her to celibacy
for the remainder of her life by making all
Ijer property forfeitable if she re-marries.
By doing these things it even raises a serious
doubt as to whether he really owns the lady,
and whether she doesn’t to some extent okn
herself. And while thus infringing the'
liberty of the male subject, it- confers on the
female one a lot of liberty which she hasn’t
previously enjoyed, but the law has hardly
yet grown accustomed to considering the
female subject as one who requires any
liberty.
* * * *
There are many arguments to be advanced
on each side of the case, and they look
almost equally convincing, though on grounds
of public policy The Bulletin regards
O’Malley’s scheme with favor. In theory,
every man has a right to dispose of his pro-
perty as he pleases. In practice, he has no
such right. Many years ago it dawned on
legislators that, if a man died leaving behind
him a large fortune, and directed that it
should be tied up in the hands of trustees to
accumulate at compound interest for an
enormous number of years, and he succeeded
in finding reliable trustees, and restricted
them to safe investments, his heir, when the
trust expired, might own one-half the earth,
or even more. Therefore, on grounds of
public safety, it put a strict limit to the
duration of such trusts, and thus decided
that a man has no absolute right to bequeath
his property as he pleases. In the present
case, it is merely proposed to carry the same
policy a little further, for equally good
reasons. In frequent instances, the man who
accumulates property does so partly by deny-
ing himself and partly by denying his wife ;
the pile is very often the result of their com-
bined labor, brains, and penuriousness, and
the law which allows the old man in all cases
to bequeath his partner’s share as well as his
own to some third party is manifestly unfair.
Against this, the other theory is that a wife
who brings nothing into the partnership, and
doesn’t contribute to making the fortune,
and who is unfaithful, and throws crockery
at the old man, and hastens his
end in Various ways, will have an equally
good lien upon his fortune. The question is
practically whether the bad old man should
have power to disinherit his deserving wife
and let her starve in her old age, because he
wants to leave all his property to some
church as a fire insurance on his depraved
soul, or out of pure cussedness, or out of
vanity because he desires to build a gorgeous
structure to his own memory, or because he
wants to endow his pet barmaid, or for other
unsavory reasons—or, on the other hand,
whether the good old man should be com-
pelled to endow the gold-washed harridan
whom he married in a moment of aberra-
tion, and who made his existence a misery
ever afterwards.
* * * *
This paper believes that he ought, as the
less of two evils. Obviously if he doesn’t
provide for her maintenance somebody else
must, and he might as well do it as anybody
else. The law requires every man to main-
tain his wife during his lifetime if he has the
means, and it is absurd for the same law to
allow him to leave her absolutely without
maintenance after his death. This is giving
him when he is dead a power which he
didn’t possess when he was alive. Also, the
law under which a man may require his
widow, possibly little more than a girl, to
remain unmarried for the rest of her life
under penalty of losing her fortune, is con-
trary to public policy. It puts a distinct
premium on immorality ; she may become
somebody’s mistress and still remain inde-
pendent, but if she becomes a wife she
is financially ruined, therefore she has a
strong cash inducement to accept the worse
alternative. The statute which, merely for
the sake of preserving the alleged right of
every citizen to do as he pleases with his
own property, permits any corpse to offer
such inducements to. any live person, is cer-
tainly carrying the law of property to
extremes. The best plea yet advanced for
the continuance of the existing system
is that the Fat Man should be allowed to get
even with his unworthy wife, if he wants to,
by leaving her penniless.. This assumes that
in cases of disagreement the Fat Man’s, wife
is always to blame—that he is always the
best judge of whether she is unworthy or
not—and that if the lady is so bad that she
shouldn’t be maintained out of his estate she
is good enough to be maintained, if neces-
sary, at the cost of somebody else.
When it is, claimed that it would be
a ? 1. ou ,trage if the law did not give every
citizen, the right to. leave his widow wholly
unproyided for on the ground that she may
deserve to be so treated, or that he may
think she does, several very large assump-
tions are thrown in,. The law is presumably
trying, to do its best for the deceased, and
the theory that the best it can do for him is
to, let him die with the pleasant conscious-
ness tlia.t his relict is left to poverty is, a
toierably low-down species of morality.
From the religious point of view, too, the
assumption, that his soul, wherever it has
gone—supposing that he has a. soul, and
assuming that it has gone any where in par-
ticular—will feel any better for having done
this is a depressing view of Christianity. Yet
either the law—which, claims to be a Chris-
tian, institution, and opens Parliament with
prayer, and makes people sweat; on, a
microbe-infected Bible, and, ask,s that the
Lord will have mercy on, the hanged, man’s,
soul-does think this, or else it doesn’t tlnnk
the deceased, has a spul, op else it doesn’t
care how, his,soul feels, Moreover, a need-
lessly-hungry widow is, npt a pleasant affair
for humane people to .contemplate, and, what-
ever her demerits, it is,questionable whether
any man has a right, for his own private
to inflict this unpicturesque
spectacle on persons who never did
him any injury. If the question is to lie
between making the man who is dead
feel annoyed—if he is capable of feeling any-
thing—at the thought that she is provided for,
and making people who are alive feel uncom-
fortable by seeing that she isn’t, the most
reasonable course seems to be for the corpse
to give way. There is no proof that he has
any knowledge of the matter, and there is
abundant proof that the other people have.
Anyhow, if there is no outrage on property
in the fact that the law doesn’t allow the live
man with means to eject his wife into a cold
world simply on his own unsupported asser-
tion that she deserves to be so ejected, or on
no assettion at all, unsupported or otherwise,
then there seems to be no reason why it
should be more regardful of the dead one.
* * * *
Premier Kingston opposes the proposed
change on the general ground that “no ne-
cessity for it has ever arisen within his
knowledge.” This, however, doesn’t neces-
sarily prove anything. Within The Bulle-
tin’s knowledge, cases have arisen in which
the want of such a law was very evident—
not many cases, certainly, but still enough
to make an argument in favor of O’Malley’s
scheme. What proportion of cases there
may be which escape both Mr. Kingston
ancl The Bulletin’s notice it is impossible
to say. At all events, if Mr. Kingston
holds that it is safe to entrust the average
man with power to leave his wife unprovided
for after his death on the ground that he
won’t misuse it, he should also be prepared
to make it optional whether he supports her
or not while he is alive on the ground that
he can be trusted not to misuse that power
also. If the law can’t trust him in the latter
case—and the Statute-book seems to assert
that it can’t—then there seems no reason
why it should trust him in the former; If
O’Malley’s proposal becomes law, however,
it is practically certain that the matter will
not end there. It is impossible for the
law to give the wife a definite claim on
the estate of the old mau deceased without
giving the children a similar claim. Fur-
ther, it is hardly possible to give the wife
a claim without ultimately giving the mistress
—where there is no wife and a man habitu-
ally lives with a woman to whom he is not
married—some kind of lien also. If the male
citizen is compelled to recognise the claims
of his wife, then to allow him to ignore those
of his mistress means authorising him to
punish her not only for her immorality but
for his own. It likewise means giving the
bad man—bad according to the canons of
conventional Grundyism—a right denied to
the (conventionally) good man, which is
wholly illogical. Further, it is an open ques-
tion whether, in cases where the property is
small, the limitation of the widow’s claim to
one-third is not almost as great an injustice
as the present system. The whole question
consists in striking a balance between the
rights of the man and the woman, and the
public on whose hands the woman may be
left, and, on the whole, The Bulletin in-
clines to give the woman the benefit of
the doubt, where there is one. As a rule,
the widower has the same prospects of earn-
ing a livelihood after his bereavement as
before; the widow, in most cases, is pro-
spectless—has no trade or profession or
occupation of her own whatever ; if she
originally had one she has usually lost it.
The joint property is generally in the hus-
band’s possession, except where he is a
fraudulent insolvent. The average woman
has no chance to provide for herself, for her
only payment is her board and her clothes.
Also, the world, which is a cold place for the
average man, is. a still colder one for the
woman, whose wages are lower and oppor-
tunities fewer, and therefore she can make
out a tolerably good claim for special con-
sideration. Consequently, The Bulletin
gives its vote to that gaudy and diffuse
American, King O’Malley, on the present
occasion, on- the general ground that the
widow of romance and the widow of reality
are two very different individuals., and that
if even a very little can be done to l prevent
the latter going out washing, so frequently it
will be a little done in the right direction.

The Half-Holiday Problem.
liie stereotyped howl now going tip re the
proposed compulsoi-y weekly half-holiday for
shop-assistants in Victoria is the loss which it
must cause to the shopkeeper. “One house in
a Melbourne suburb,” writes a bilious bar-
facker, “ will lose in turnover at least £IOO,OUO
pei annum if this Saturday craze is carried out;
and at least a-third of that amount if Wednes-
day comes into force.” A good many other
people hold forth in the same strain, the evi-
dent assumption being that if shops shut up for
one half-day the money they now take on that
half-day will be altogether lost. In other
words, if all the shops closed one-half of every
day the aggregate takings would go down 50
per cent ; people would only eat and drink half
as much, and wear half as much clothing,
boots and hats, and read half as many books,
and everybody would save half his wages forth-
with, And, on the other hand, it follows that
it all shops remained open night and day for
seven days a week, the takings would be
doubled, and to do this people would eat and
cnnk and wear twice as much, and every man
would smoke two cigars or pipes at once, and
have four boots on simultaneously, and spend
twice as much money as he has, and sleep
m two beds every night. The real proba-
ii !i tI^ fc no ? alam ity will happen at
all, things will remain just as they
are. The average person spends all the
money he has, and he will most likely go on
doing so whether the shops are open six days
oi only s)r. It won’t make any difference in
ins appetite, or make liis boots last any longer,
or limit his wants in any way. The bogey
about the immense loss of trade through a shop-
keepers weekly half - holiday is about the
poorest-looking spectre which Fat Man lias
pioduced up to date. If the loss through
closing on Saturday afternoon will ruin the
shopkeeper, the chances are that the loss
through not opening on Sunday must have
r^ n ? . " lm a l rea dy, and in that case the mis-
chief is already done.

Private Executions.
Thr evening newspapers of Australia, without'
any exception that The Bulletin knows of,,
enthusiastically advocate the employment of
capital punishment—apparently because execu-
tions take place in tlie morning and add enor-
mously to circulation. Hence it has been more
than once proposed by opponents of such
punishment that the execution, while it remains
a legal penalty, should not only be private in
respect to general spectators, but also in regard
to the press. The gain hoped for from this
change is that evening journals, instead of
championjng the death penalty would, finding


the sensationalism gone, come round to the
opposite view. It is interesting to note that the
latest executions in England have been carried
out in the enforced absence of the newspaper
representatives. Reporters applied at the
Walton gaol for admission to witness the hang-
ing of Elijah Winstanley, the Wigan mur-
derer, but were informed that in accordance
with a new regulation the press was excluded
from such functions. Doctors, officials and
special officers of the Crown alone attended,
and the hoisting of a black flag over the gaol
wall informed the public that the tragedy was
completed. The community should, as a
general thing, stand up for the presence of
the press when public acts are being performed.
It is conceivable, but barely so, that a knot
of officials and professional men might conspire
to break the law or hush up some huge blunder
in the method of hanging if the press were not
present to expose them. But this is remote
enough to be treated as a mere imagination.
Besides, the civilisation which condemned
hanging in the presence of the general public
should logically condemn it in presence of men
whose sole business at the foot of the gallows is
to word-picture to the life the terrible spec-
tacle. The thrilling letterpress of the evening
journals is no more a public service when
spaced about a criminal on the gallows than it
would be if it described the muscular and other
contortions of a patient under the knife of an
hospital surgeon. The journals which accept
without chagrin exclusion from the operating-
rooms of hospitals while serious cases are dealt
with should similarly accept exclusion from
gaol-yards on hanging mornings. I his is a
reform which the Australias should promptly
give effect to. The British individuals who
once delighted to witness public executions go
now to the evening journals on hanging days to
appease their fierce appetites. In the words of
the graphic penman they behold the victim
quaking, kicking, exuding foam and blood and
otherwise evidencing supreme agony, and,
while reading, they feel as happy as their
predecessors did in watching an execution ftom
the street. The alleged civilisation which put
down the one should long ago have put down
the other.
“The Christs of Trade.”
The doctrine of Common Employment arrived
with Queen Victoria in 1837, and is another
blessed proof that “Rule Britannia” was
written by Moses, and must be accepted by all
men. The doctrine, roughly-stated, is that
a servant impliedly takes upon himself the
risks arising from the negligence of a fellow-
servant. The province of Victoria has
an Act which draws some of the teeth
of this ravenous doctrine, but it will expire
with the current session, unless continued by
legislation. Meanwhile, Irvine has introduced
a Bill, for the second time, to put the statutory
obligations of employers on a humane basis.
Last session Irvine’s speech, as reported in
Hansard, gave in vivid colors the brutal
tendencies of the Common Law in interpreting
the rights of employers, and it is to be hoped
that Parliament will this session put the law in
line with sanity and humanity. Randolph
Bedford spoke in certain verses of the “ Christs
of Trade ” whose blood, in too literal fashion,
consecrates buildings, canals, and works gene-
rally. Eleven men died to make a Yankee
assurance palace in Collins-street. Statistics
show how many deaths there are to a mile in
railway-making. Who pays for the death of a
workman? As a rule, the inevitable widow lias
no case at law, and no money to bring it to trial
if it existed. The contractor, possibly a good
fellow enough, sends along a five-pound note —
the fellow-workmen subscribe a few shillings.
Then it is all forgotten. After all, one widow
more or less is a mere detail in the blue-books
of the country—and if there are young child-
ren, the Lord will provide. Is the Irvine
remedy sufficient ? Would it not simplify
matters to make the employer responsible for
every death or injury by accident on
his works unless clearly d <e to the
personal negligence of the victim fixing
a lump - sum payable in all cases ? The
risk would be insurable, and would simply
make a small (and just) addition to the capital
cost of the work. The destruction of life that
occurs with painful regularity on big contracts
should be reckoned as part of the cost of the
undertaking.

The V ic. Full Court in the case of Morrison
v. N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co., has
just affirmed the decision of Judge Hodges on
a point of much constitutional importance. An
attempt was made in this case to foist the Im-
perial legislation with reference to winding-up
on to the self-governing provinces without their
consent. The argument was based on the fact
that the Imperial Parliament had had the im-
pudence to expressly apply similar bankruptcy
provisions to the provinces and on the similarity
between winding-up and bankruptcy. But, as
Judge Holroyd pointed out, “ this reasoning,
though specious, was quite fallacious.” “ The
court was asked to say that the . section in the
English act imposed a new law on the colonies
in derogation of the legislative powers pos-
sessed by the colonies which enjoyed self-
government.” This statement puts the
numerous acts of impertinence of the British
Parliament in extending statutes on all kinds of
subjects to the “self-governing” provinces with-
out even saying “ by your leave,” into glaring
relief. When will the due resentment be felt
by the provinces, and the abuse accordingly
promptly stopped ?

Switzerland should make a profitable object-
lesson to many who exaggerate the difficulties
of compensating expropriated publicans—if,
as a matter of expediency (not of justice)
publicans should be compensated at all. When
that country determined to make a State mono-
poly of the” sale and manufacture of spirits it
found 1400 private distilleries i in operation
Within its boundaries. A few of these it
retained as-the nucleus of the State establish-
ment, and the rest it compulsorily abolished.
Over'l.SOO were summarily closed, but in every
case under a scheme of compensation which
neither the State nor the expropriated indivi-
duals find fault with. The total cash the State
undertook to pay amounted to <£236,000,
spreading the instalments over a period of ten
years. The last instalment will be due in 1898,
and from that day forward Switzerland will
have a clean sheet. The per contra side is
quite as interesting and instructive. Beginning
with a comparatively small annual profit, the
State, during the last three or four years, has
cleared over <£200,000 per annum per medium
of its spirit monopoly.
The law reform scheme, introduced the other
day by S. A. Government, promises, if it doesn’t
come to grief in the graveyard of the Upper
House, to be about the best and most business-
like thing of its kind Australia has yet seen.
It provides that solicitors and barristers shall
not be required to know any dead and putrefied
languages or have any university degree ; their
qualifications are to be simply two years’ office
experience and ability to pass an examination
in law. The law is to be open equally to
either sex. Barristers can sue for their fees if
irecessary, and barristers and solicitors can be
sued for damages for negligence if they fail to
attend to a case which they have undertaken.
No appeal is to be allowed in cases where less
than <£loo is involved unless they involve
some other question of right besides the money
at stake. No case shall be re-heard on the
mere ground that evidence was improperly
admitted, unless the evidence so admitted had
a direct bearing on the case. No case is
to be removed from the Lower Courts to the
more expensive Supreme Court except by con-
sent of both parties, and even then the party
applying for the removal bears the extra cost.
The Courts are instructed to decide on the
merits of the case without regard to “ techni-
calities or questions of form, practice or pro-
cedure.” The mode of appeal is simplified, and
in cerlain cases, notably probate cases, lawyers
are not allowed to appear. When two persons
have a legal matter in dispute, instead of start-
ing a lawsuit they may write out, and both sign,
a statement of the case, and the matter shall be
decided without appeal by the local Court for a
charge of £l, or by the Supreme Court for <£3—
the parties may choose whichever Court they
please. These are the more important provi-
sions, and they all seem to tend towards making
the law a little more simple and a good deal
less expensive than it is at present.
YiO. Leg. Assembly passed a good Bill last week
to amend the Local Government Act so that
non-payment of rates shall not disfranchise the
constituents of a municipal council. The argu-
ment used against the measure by Brother
Madden was that it extended
the fashionable doctrine that those who contributed
nothing- to the taxation should have the largest share of
representation.
But this objection would only be cogent if for-
feiture of the vote under present conditions
nullified the responsibility to taxation. In the
great multitude of instances where disfranchise-
ment takes place under the present law the
default does not lie with the person who loses
his vote, but with some member of the landlord-
class who has contracted to pay the rates, and
has been permitted to evade the duty for a
time by the carelessness or indifference of the
municipal officers. In any case, the munici-
pality has a continual lien on property in arrears
for rates, and the disfranchisement of the occu-
pier is a punishment altogether out of propor-
tion to the offence.

again. No sane policeman would have dared
to arrest a female with influence at her back,
or probably any male with his wits about him,
on the so-called evidence adduced. The Law
for the Rich shrugged its shoulders and waited
while the girl was in gaol, without even a chance
to hear what evidence might be being fossicked
out or manufactured against her. The fact
that “ the Crown,” with all its police machinery
and malignant system of trying by hook or by
crook to “prove its case,” couldn’t allege any-
thing at all to the satisfaction of a jury, against
an undefended girl, without means or wit-
nesses, conveys a whole drama of significance.
In the first place, no magistrate should have
committed this girl for trial —for ten weeks in
gaol! In the second place, a committing
magistrate should have the power to liberate
accused poor persons on their own recognisances.
If they are too poor to command bail, they are
too poor to bolt from the law, for they have no
feathers to fly with; and, in any case, they
could be required to report themselves once a
week to the local police, if such a severity were
deemed necessary. Even if one wretch out of a
dozen or so did get away the country could
stand it, and the law wouldn’t, be hurt so badly
in its reputation as it is by the fact that it im-
prisoned an innocent girl for ten weeks, and
possibly made her—what ?
Dear Bulletin, —A word or two about the
Deceased Wife’s Sister’s Bill, which has at last
become law in England. There were, and still
are, some good grounds for the opposition it
met with for years. Man and woman are by
marriage made one. The Churches say so, and
the physiologists admit that, after some years
of cohabitation, a certain approach to unity in
duality is established between husband and
wife —the latter, however, as is natural, being
the larger shareholder in the unity, or, in other
words, the woman absorbs more of the nature
of the man than the man does that of the
woman. Therefore, marriage between a man
and the sister of his dead wife would seem to
be something not unlike a new and original
species of diluted incest. Then suppose that
there were children by the first wife, and chil-
dren' by her sister afterwards, what relations
would they bear to each other ? They would be
more than half-brothers and sisters—say, three-
quarter brothers and sisters. Genealogy is a
sufficiently difficult science as it is without this
new complication being dragged into it. These
latter are but small objections, however. A
stronger than any lies in the fact that the new
law is likely to play the devil with a good many
happy homes. A wife who has a pretty sister
living with her, will, in future, be liable to look
with a jealous eye on any little passages of
family affection—any otherwise natural kisses
of good-bye or welcome —exchanged between
her husband and that sister. She will suspect
that the latter is waiting to step into her shoes
when she dies, and is paving the way in good
time. And then there will be trouble. This
state of affairs will, however, be productive of
one advantage to unmarried girls with married
sisters. The latter will work harder than half-
a-dozen match-making mothers to provide the
former with husbands of their own. lam
thinking there will be some new and picturesque
material for the dramatist after the Deceased
Wife’s Sister’s Act has been working for a year
or two. And now, in conclusion. Women are
demanding, and rapidly acquiring, all the rights
and privileges of men. The next thing we
shall hear is that they aie clamoring for a
Deceased Husband’s Brother’s Act. Why not ?
What is sauce for the gander is sauce for the
goose. What does the goose say ? — CreEVE
Roe. [Query: Why haven’t these dreadful
things happened already in countries like Aus-
tralia, where the D.W.S. Act has been tried ?
And if the average wife finds her average hfls-
band kissing her pretty sister too extensively,
would she wait to hunt up the Statute Book
and discover if the D.W.S. Act was in force
before throwing something, or would she throw
it on the spot? —Ed. B.]
“ Stumpjumper” to The Bulletin :
Deal’ Bulletin, —It is not the N.S.W. “special-areas”
holders alone who clamor for re-appraisement of their
hard bargains ; and, as a matter of fact, they have not as
good a case to present as the ordinary selectors of C.P.
and C.L. The “ special-area” victims did take up then-
holdings “ with their eyes open,” as the opponents of re-
appraisement are so fond of reminding them, but the
ordinary conditional purchaser has to “go it blind” on
three-fourths of his area, as the rent of the lease is not
stated until he has bound himself to accept the land or
forfeit his deposit. This disposes of the fatuous argu-
ment “ they knew what they had to pay ” of the city
land-expert whenever the question of all-round re-
appraisement crops up. In your par. on the subject,
Aug. 22, you write:—“The clamor of the agitators is
tolerably comprehensive: (1) District surveyors, who
make valuations, are not experts in valuing; (2) land
boards decline to receive expert evidence as to values ;
(3) the expert evidence that is received is unreliable. Yet
it only means, on behalf of the proposed beneficiaries :
Holders don’t want to pay anything more than they can
help, and don’t want to pay that much until they are
forced to do so. But they hold on to the land and din
noises into the eai-s of local members.” To No. 1, 2 and 3
any settler would reply, “ True enough.” That we don’t
want to pay any more than we can help applies equally to
holders of*even shares in a newspaper—postage, for in-
stance—but that we successfully hold on to the land is
emphatically denied by the ever-increasing number of
small holdings transferred to the banks. But what a
subject it is ! A whole issue of Tim Bulletin would
barely suffice to do it justice from the settlers’ point of
view, and it is easier for George Reid to crawl through
the eye of a needle than for the man on the land to get a
fair chance of explaining why he doesn’t stay there.
Here’s a point that throws a little light on the subject of
re-appraisement. A number of small holders, when mak-
ing up their “landaninkum” papers, put down the value
of their conditionally-purchased land at £1 per acre sim-
ply because the Government valued it at that price, and
as the amount paid up was too small to bring them over
the exemption they didn’t trouble to dispute it. By and
bye, when they have paid a few more instalments of the
purchase-money and are liable for land-tax, there will be
trouble. In the meantime, the valuers appointed by the
department to value every holding, however small, are in
a dilemma. For instance, one of them, trying to do his
work fairly, remarked to me : “ I expect I’ll get into trou-
ble over this job. Some of the cookies in this district
have put their stony ridges down at £1 an acre in then-
returns, and honestly the land isn’t worth more than 55,”
I fancy if the Minister compares that valuer’s report with
the owners’ returns he, too, will think “ the expert evi-
dence received is unreliable.”
After all, Mr. Greene, the Inglewood (Vic.)
coroner, did good service, the other day, when
he ordered that hard-up septuagenarian suicide
to be buried by night. For he has succeeded,
as he could have effectually donq in no other
way, in directing attention to the fact that not
in Victoria alone but in N.S.W. also every
coroner is pretty well a law unto himself. The
principal Act seems to belong to the reign of
Edward 1., and for the rest the delightfully
uncertain common law is relied upon. In
N.S.W. there have been almost countless cir-
culars to coroners suggesting what different
Attorneys-General and Under-Secretaries have
regarded as the proper courses ; but in several
cases these circulars come into conflict.
Wherefore the coroner exercises that wide
discretion which he is continually told he
possesses, and does as he darned well pleases.
But as he is apt to do some absurd things it is
time to ask why he should have all this discre-
tion. Way back, being the direct representa-
tive of the Crown, charged with inquiring into
sudden or mysterious deaths, he was naturally
given the Crown’s freedom ; but now, as his re-
lations with the Crown are somewhat less
intimate, and as the functions are fairly circum-
scribed, there seems no good reason why the
law shouldn’t step in and save the coroner from
making a greater ass of himself than need be.
Last week at Sydney Quarter Sessions several
Chinamen were together indicted before Judge
Docker for running a lottery. Some of them
pleaded guilty and were fined in various sums
hot exceeding Jl5. The others, however,
defended the case. During the conduct of the
trial their counsel raised certain points of law
which the Judge over-ruled; on a verdict of
guilty, these points were reserved for the Full
Court. This practice, it is hardly necessary to
say, is an absolute right conferred on accused
people by the Legislature, a right which should
be jealously guarded, seeing that the only
appeal open, in criminal cases, is one on a
point of law’. Many cases will suggest them-
selves in w'hich the' Appeal Court has stood
between a careless Judge and his victims.
According to the newspaper version of the
case, Judge Docker holds strange views con-
cerning appeals and the rights of prisoners.
When he sentenced the Chinamen, he is re-
ported to have carefully explained that, “ since
they were going to appeal, he would impose
a sentence of three months’ imprisonment.”
Three months for blasphemously daring to
question the ruling of Judge Docker !
Judge Docker, it would really seem, takes
it as a personal insult that a citizen, even
when his liberty depends on it, should not
be satisfied with the Judge’s opinion on a
matter of law ! The only real way of avoiding
these judicial eccentricities is to allow the jury,
who find a man guilty, to say how guilty he is,
and to fix his punishment within statutory
limits.

School for Scandal ” did a three-night
season at Sydney Lyceum, and the go and energy
ot the grand old comedy were a relief after the
slow, gradual wretchedness of “ Camille. ” Bellew
made a brilliant Charles Surface ; this paper looks
back down the long vista of the years and decides
upon its oath, taken before a sober J.P., that
Bellew is the best C. Surface of its recollection.
LLis over-floured visage and painted lips make
him look more or less unhealthy at all times, and
his Surface was a rather spectral visitant, but
beyond that there were no complaints to make.
1 he Lady .teazle of Mrs. Potter was also too much
noured and painted, and the paint was mostly worn
as a flush about the eyes ? and a lady with a pink
tore head and a pallid chin is never a great success.
Also, she successfully wrecked' practically every
eature of the part by her elocution, and made
Lady JLeazle a gaudy ruin from almost every
Vlew -. In fact, there was hardly an un-
kind thing which could have been done" to Lady
Teazle that Mrs Potter did not do. She was
practically the only failure, however. Scot Inglis’s
Joseph was not his best work, but it was very good
none the less ; Wilson Forbes and Cathcart were
two excellent old men; and the rest of the co.
spread itself for a great effort, and the result was
gratifying. Miss Linda Raymond in particular
deserved to have some kind remarks made about
her, and they are hereby made accordingly.
Charlotte Corday ” did a one-night season
on Saturday, after a patient audience had waited
for the late Mrs. Potter till nearly 8.15, and the
production was splendidly mounted and well
played by everybody except the heroine. Mrs.
potter didn t once rise to the tragic intensity of
the character—her eyes rose, and her right hand
and sometimes her hair, but the lady herself didn’t.
When she read the thrilling story of how Judith
struck IMofernes she made the Jewish maiden
land the oppressor with all her mite—she didn’t
hit him with anything larger in the Potter version.
Also her irritating accent was more aggravating
even than usual; when she rushed at Marat and
called him assossm,” the general feeling for a
moment was that she had called him “sausage.”
As usual, she wriggled in wholly unnatural
positions all through, and rolled her eyes
unspeakably, and extended her hands hori-
zontally with the fingers outspread fan-
tashion, and talked with an agonisingly slow
drip of conversation, and, in short, she failed in a
wholly original plan of her own. Mrs. Potter’s
failures are not at all commonplace; they are
quaint, weird, bizarre, suggestive of the yellow
decadent school of art, and arouse ideas of an
impressionist picture that has tried to do some-
thing without success. “ Charlotte Corday ” is
the sort of play that Mrs. Potter delights in. The
first two acts are mostly devoted to the gradual
soubupheaval of Charlotte; the last one is a
wholly superfluous affair in which she strikes
attitudes and is an afflicted saint in the limelight
Marat supplies the life and color of the whole
affair. Charlotte is mainly a pale, honorable
speotre against a background of tragedy.
Bellew s Marat was a fine conception.
Probably Marat never received justice from his-
tory, but Bellew s Dictator, with his moist, husky
voice, like the voice of one with a dish-cloth in his
throat, and the catarrhal snuffle of his long, red
sad, damp nose, and his diseased countenance
and his crab-like gait satisfied the popular eoncep-
tion to a nicety. Moreover, he had a blood-curdling
laugh that made the hair stand on end. and a
wild-beast snarl of unparalleled malignity. The
fact that Bellew s Marat took his bath with his
clothes on was, perhaps, an unavoidable anachron-
ism. And the fact that Marat has been reviled
through 100 years for his dirt among other things
and yet died washing himself to the bitter end is
another anachronism. Scot Inglis’s David, Mont-
gomerys Adam Lux, and the two old men repre-
sented by Forbes and Cathcart, were all admir-
able, a,nd Misses Ida Hamilton and Polly Emery
tilled in two small parts in the background well
enough for all purposes. To-night (Thursday) the
co. breaks out into the “ Lady of Lyons.” It
tnes Camille ” again on Friday, and on Satur-
day in at Goodwin is due with “ The Nominee.”
* * * *
B. Holt made a wise choice of the “ Prodi-
gal Daughter” for the beginning of his new
season at Sydney Royal. There are great splashes
of color m this drama, and much electric-light and
nrst-class carpet, and it is full of innumerable
horse, and, above all, there is no dull spot in it
from beginning to end. But the odor of horse
which pervades it throughout is the keynote of the
production. Even in the dining-room scene of
Act 1. the irrelevant horse puts his head in at the
Window to show that he is giving his moral sup-
port to the afflicted father, and Acts III. and IV.
are practically all horse. When the pervading
animal is not actually present in the cats-meat,
Bland Holt is sitting on a bucket and thinking of
him, or else Miserable Jim is being bribed to nobble
him. Miss Elizabeth Watson does her part
as the girl who went wrong more brightly than
usual, and Miss Frances Ross does hers as well as
usual, and, barring one astounding hat, she is a
feature in the landscape. Mr. and
Mrs. Bland, Baker, Charles Brown, and Norman
Harford (as the two baleful persons dressed in
the top-hat of iniquity and long black coat of
guile, who plot against the horse) all hold up the
vast superstructure of the show in a capable
manner. On Monday night the Grand National
put up a record in stage-steeplechasing,; the race-
course was kicked to shivers in a fine realistic
tashion, and the air was full of broken hurdle and
tom vocabulary, and turf and wrecked fence, and
capsized jockey and horse standing on his ear,
and when Baker furiously drove “the Jook ” to
victory over the ruins, and manager Chris.
Simonsen madly urged his pallid steed
through everything and got a place, the
general impression was that it was a fine large
race, and the audience would be glad to see
another just like it. If the race-scene could be
put on like that or made even a little madder
every night, and Chris. Siinonsen’s pale horse
could be guaranteed to come pounding round the
corner regularly, kicking great hunks off the uni-
verse, and the Jook could be relied on to arrive
the racecourse in his mouth, the
Prodigal Daughter ” should be good for a three
months run. Business is rather prosperous at the
-tvoyal, so far, and the black-hatted betrayer will
climb surreptitiously in at the window and poison
his own horse by mistake till further notice.

Mr. Hodson : s season at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) closes at the end of this week, the last
four nights being filled in with “Under the Gas-
light,' which arrives too late for notice. The new
drama is also “ by arrangement with ” Billolloway,

The kinetomatograph, which is the kine-
toscope writ large, was on private exhibition at
AimG 6 ? , one n, & ht last week under Joseph
M Mahon s management. The difference between
the new thing with the long name and the old
thing with the name that isn’t much shorter is
that the latter shows the dog-fight in miniature
through a glass, while the former reflects it full
size on a sheet, so that you see the whole disturb-
ance just as it really was. If it is a really cordial
and enthusiastic dog-fight the effect is very gratify-
ing ; in fact, it is almost as good as the real thing,
barring the yapping, and as you can have the same
nght as often as you like without wearing out the
dogs, it makes the pleasure go further. The new
invention is to be on public exhibition shortly.

Great preparations are being made in Syd-
ney to give Kowalski a fitting send off. His
pupils, headed by Miss Beatrice Griffiths, are
workmg assiduously and intend to play Kowalski’s
Marche Hongroise ” in company. The pro-
minent artists of Sydney have taken the matter
up, and a bumper attendance is expected. It is
now known that Kowalski will not return to Aus-
tralia. Weighted with years, and discouraged by
the apathy of Australian audiences, he will pass
the evening of his life in his beloved Prance.

The aroma of faded notoriety that clings
to “ Called Back ” drew a great crowd to Mel-
bourne Royal on Saturday night. When George
Rignold first staged this unusual story at the
Opera House, 11 years ago, Hugh Conway’s novel
was the “Trilby” of the period. The girl who
dropped her memory in a moment of horror may
be said to have bequeathed her mantle to the
weak-minded model who just now travels around
on bare feet. Paolo Macari was her Svengali. To
the rising generation, knowing little more of
Pauline and Paolo than the echo of their names,
Called Back ’ is submitted on its merits, and
portions of, the dialogue fall so heavily on the
gallery-boy s spirits that he would scorn the play
but for sundry thrilling incidents, the first
of which has been copied pretty closely
(without acknowledgment) in the prologue
“°r< vPj 6 J >o,n * STrian - ” The prologue murder in
Called Back ” happens right under the noses of
two virtuous characters who can’t afterwards tes-
tify to what occurred. The heroine, after wit-
nessing the gory deed, forgets all about it, whereas
her sweetheart retains full consciousness of having
been present at a criminal performance which he
couldn t see. For the next two years or so the
young man, having recovered his sight, gazes
sadly at the dear girl whose memory fled, and the
girl walks in and out of the room iri an Ophelian-
Inlby dream, wondering what is, was, and ever
can be the matter. A lot of other sore affliction is
compressed into the drama. The girl’s maternal
uncle, Dr. Cenari, terminates an absurd career
Of plotting against oppression—he’s not at all par-
ticular as to which oppression he plots against—
by dying outside a Siberian dungeon, surrounded
by cold snow and icicles. A sniffing landlady is
introduced solely for the purpose of mentioning
that square gin is the only specific for her awful
spasms, and the spy Macari, who has been send-
lng most of his friends to gaol and death, gets
slaughtered at the finish. Macari, contracting
nis legs in death agony, like a newly-decapitated
rooster, .is the moral lesson for the evening. With
the exception of a cheerful young married couple,
who really don’t count, all the people in “Called
-Back are ill-fated civilians, or soldiers and
warders acquainted with deep distress

vellian person, very suggestive of the Rignoldian
devil in I< aust. He has ceased to act the lady-
iailer, yet he isn’t such a suspicious-looking char-

oterlmg Whites idea of Dr. Ceneri is a poor,

The Wild-Cat Column.
The fact that the Queensland National Bank is
in a bad way seems to be now fully acknowledged. Last
week the Government appointed a committee of three to
investigate and report on its condition, and also as to
whether the Government should assist it, and, if so, how,
w * la *' extent? The committee is to report as to
the bank’s solvency “ assuming that the assets are realised
judiciously and not by forced sales.” There is so much
scope here for putting fancy prices on securities on the
ground that these prices are what they might sell for if
realised “ judiciously,” and saying afterwards that the
utter failure to fetch anything like the alleged values
arose from the fact that they weren’t sold judiciously
that this clause is a distinct intimation that the
Q.N.B. is to be let down very easily. As a further
guarantee that it will be let down easily the committee as
originally created consisted of two shareholders and a
gentleman who manages a concern of which one or more
big Q.N.B. shareholders are alleged to be the bosses—in
other words, the committee of three apparently consisted
of two shareholders and a shareholders’ servant. Also, it
is alleged that one of the three is heavily interested in a
co. which has a big overdraft in the Q.N.8., which over-
draft, of course, would be called up in case of liquidation.
Under all these and other circumstances the committee
was evidently intended to report that the Q.N.B. is quite
solid and only needs the State to take up £2,500,000
worth of shares and ask no questions to make everything
go beautifully. Even the Kanaka Parliament, however,
could not quite stand Nelson’s committee, and the name
of the Auditor-General was added to it so that it wouldn’t
be entirely a shareholders’ affair.
If the present Q. Government could be trusted
to run a bank honestly, or even with only an ordinary
amount of dishonesty, it would certainly be much better
for it to come to the Q.N.B.’s assistance than for thatcon-
<*">*> «*»* an pther panic. To do the thing honestly it
should have a rigid, independent audit—not an audit by
shareholders or their agents—and find out what the
shortage, if there is one, really amounts to. Then it
should make the shareholders pay up the shortage so far
as their paid and callable capital will go, and after that is
+i? n r> l^T^ ou almost certainly be cheaper to take over
the Q.N.B. as a State institution and stand the rest of the
loss than to have a collapse that would shake the province
to its foundations. The State, with any kind of sane
management, would make up the deficit, even if it was a
large one, in course of time out of the profits, and there-
after the institution would be a source of revenue. But
apparently the idea is to decide, after a feeble investiga-
tion, that there is really no loss worth speaking about,
and put a lot of Government money into the concern to
prop it up, and leave the same old crowd who ran the
institution on the rocks in the past to run it in the future.
h xTn°f ds ’J hesl^nsare that >s intended to save
the Q.N.B. for the sake of the Fat Man shareholders who
have been grinding dividends out of it almost up to the
date of the appeal to Parliament, and not (as in the case
of the 8.N.Z.) for the sake of the community.

The Maoriland Govt. Insurance Department
presents a healthy-looking report for 1895. The total
lnc ? rn ®, including consideration for annuities, is
£266,032, against £254,204 in ’94. Total income, includ-
es' I , r !i e , reS t. on mvestments, is £386,012 against £369,479
fo . r , if’ 4 - On* of this revenue, £164,006 is saved and
io d , e oo t o 0 J he mu nds ’ raising them to the splendid total of
£2,428,245. The expenses are 19 per cent, of premiums
against 22 per cent, last year, and are only about 13 per
cnm • to * al i ncome - Of the accumulated funds,
£916,000 is in State debentures, and £167,000 in the
debentures of local bodies—securities whose value, unlike
that of the mortgages which form the vast bulk of Aus-
tralian insurance investments, are easily ascertained and
which contain few possibilities of undetected depreciation.
Interest overdue is only £3477, which, on an annual in-

manag-ed institution of its kind with about the safest class
of investments m Australasia.
The A.M.P. Society publishes a batch of figures
—with most of the important ones left out—comparing
f^ le State Insurance Department, and the
S.M. Herald reprints them with the statement that the
latter s business is thereby shown to be “ going to the
Since the A.M.P. invites comparison, it is a fair
thing to contrast the two offices in some respects which it
has omitted. The M.L. Government Dept.’s income from
premiums alone increased £7566 over the previous year;
A.M.P. (which is.six times the size, and does business in
seven provinces instead of one) increased £20,000. M.L.
ofSSr, ’ ncome from investments increased
£4700; A.M.P.’s decreased £4200. M.L. Dept.’s renewal
premiums increased £11,000; A.M.P.’s, with nearly six
times the business, increased £12,000 only. M.L. Dept,
lost £35,000 by surrenders; A.M.P., with six times the
business, lost about 12 times as much, £375,000. M.L.
Dept, has £3477 interest in arrear—a most important
matter, as unoollected interest always suggests dubious
securities; A.M.P. has more than eleven times as much.
M.L. Dept, saved and added to its funds £164,000 during
the year ; A.M.P. Society saved £727,000—if it had saved
as large a proportion of its income the amount should
have been over £BOO,OOO. The M.L. Govt. Dept, is bur-
dened with £32,400 worth of foreclosed property (which
is always a possible source of loss); the A.M.P. has
£538,000 worth—nearly 17 times as much. Also, though
the expenses of the M.L. Dept, are heavier—chiefly be-
cause it is a newer and smaller concern—they decreased 3
per cent. last year, while those of the A.M.P. slightly in-
creased. Under all these circumstances, the State De-
partment can afford to compare with the A.M.P.—even
though the latter is the biggest and seems the best of the
private societies of Australasia—and has decidedly the
best of the comparison in many respects. Also it has a
state guarantee behind it, which makes it safer than the
best private institution, and no private office of its own
age can come within gunshot of its record.

The Indemnity Fire and Marine Insurance Co.
for the year ended 30th June shows a loss of £1328 com-
pared with a profit of £386 last year and £3581 the year
previous. Last year, having made £386, the Indemnity 00.
spent £lOOO in paying a dividend ; this time the situation
is too painful and the dividend is passed altogether. The
Indemnity is a concern whose otherwise good prospects
are ruined by its expense account. Last year its net
premium income was £10,968, and it blewed £5015 of this
m working* expenses. Therefore, if the losses come any-
where near 60 per cent, of the premiums—which is a
thing that is bound to happen very often—the co. can’t
nearly pay its way out of revenue, let alone pay a div. To
earn a decent dividend losses have to keep down to about
40 per cent., which is too much to hope for. The co. has
£27,946 of investments, and the interest received for the
year was £512, or about £1 17s. per cent, per annum.
This lamentable figure seems to imply that something
like half of the investments are paying no interest at all,

South Australian Insurance Co. shows a profit
of £5250 for the year, of which only £329 is made out of
the insurance business, the rest being interest on invest-
ments. AlO per cent, dividend absorbs £6260, so the eo.
deliberately and of malice aforethought divides £lOOO
more than it earns. Last year it made £5854, all from in-
terest on investments, the insurance business showing a
loss of £25 ; and paid a 15 per cent, dividend (£9375), or
about £3500 more than its earnings. Unless business im-
proves materially the hunger for huge divs. at any price
will badly shake even this strong concern sooner or later.
* * * *
The Bank of Victoria announces that a third 10
per cent, instalment of its reconstructed deposits will be
released on 12th December. The Victoria has now
apparently settled down to release a 10 per cent, instal-
ment (equal to about £500,000) every three months, and
at this rate it should shortly be free of its troubles. The
present release clears off all liabilities up to 1898.
* * * *
A notable feature of the Vic. Companies Bill
is the provision that all companies must file statements
of their position made up to 31st March of each year.
This should break up the “ balancing trick.” When the
banks make up their balance-sheets at different dates the
one that is balancing can borrow- a lot of cash for a day or
two from the one that isn’t, and thus make the propor-
tion of specie to liabilities look very much better. Then
when the lending bank’s turn comes the other concern
can give it a hand if necessary. In this manner £lOO,OOO
of coin can appear in so many balance-sheets that it has
no difficulty in looking like half-a-million if necessary.
* • *
N.S.W. revenue-returns for August show an in-
crease of £36,000 as compared with those for Aug.,’9s.
This one month’s revenue, however, includes the biggest
part (£110,000) of the year’s income-tax. Leaving out the
increase on income-tax, which is a new source of revenue,
and the decrease on Customs which is caused by tariff-
reductions, and dealing only with the sources of revenue
which remain unchanged, the result is an increase of
£2284. School-fees, curiously enough, account for all the
increase. The big decreases are on land-revenue, excise
(from which it would appear that the local beer-industry
isn’t feeling well), posts and telegraphs, and Sydney water
and sewage rates, which the decrease of population has
struck heavily.
* * • •
Maoriland State Loan Department proposes to
increase the amount which may be advanced on & single
security; to make advances on suburban as well as
country lands ; and to make fixed loans (i.e., loans repay-
able in one sum on a fixed date) as well as loans repayable
by annual instalments. Probably a little later on it will
be decided to lend on town and city properties as well.
There seems no reason why it shouldn’t do all these things,
although a wild howl is arising about the departure from
the original plan, which was that of “assisting the
farmers.” The Department should be run partly to pro-
vide cheap money, partly to make profit for the State,
and if it can make profit out of loans on city lands it
should do so. There is more risk of loss by depreciation
on fixed loans than on those repayable by instalments
where the amount at stake is always diminishing, but, as
the Department won’t spend its profits in dividends as a
private co. does, it will have an ever-increasing reserve to
meet all risks. And, if no impecunious Treasurer is
allowed to grab this reserve for any purpose, it should go
on expanding till the Department can keep its business
going by lending its own accumulated capital and be alto-
gether independent of borrowed money. Then it will be
able to reduce its interest to 3 per cent., and “cheap
money ” will have arrived in earnest.
