The Bank of Australasia reports by cable that
its profits for the half-year are £40,569, against £41,158
and £40,854 for the two previous periods. A 5 per cent,
dividend accounts for £40,000 of this, and £569 is carried
forward, making the balance to credit of profit and loss
£12,222.
* * * *
The Westralian revenue for August was
£248,611. Last August it was £114,309. What a boom
they are having! And the amazing part of it is that all
this prosperity is built on gold, yet the increase alone in
the month’s revenue as compared with August, ’95, is
more than the value of all the gold found in the pro-
vince for the month.

• * * *
Melbourne Postal Enquiry has reached another
stage. The principal charge is that every possible job in
tho machinery department was given to a certain person
with a German name, and that he charged from two to
eight times a fair price. The other day an official, said to
be the special friend of the alleged extortionate German,
got into the box to show that the bills were all right.
One item of £7 10s. he claimed was especially moderate ;
the brass and labor cost £2, and making the tools to do
the job with cost £5 18s., and the German kept the tools
afterwards. Now the Board is rubbing its head violently
and wondering if the department was charged for making
a fresh lot of tools on every job.
* * * *
At North Sydney, supposed to be a suburb that,
thanks to its new railway, has kept up remarkably well
despite the general collapse, there was offered the other
day a piece of land for which £25 a foot was declined a
very few years ago. A few allotments fetched £B, and
even at that figure there was hardly any demand.
* * * •
So far, M.L. Government has resumed. 28
estates for subdivision, and, so far as the experiment has
gone, the rents received from settlers equal £4 15s. per
cent, on the money sunk. As the said money only cost
about 4 per cent., the “ruinous Socialistic experiment”
still keeps its head above water.
* * <1 *
Vic. Pull Court rules that when you sell shares,
and pass them on, with a transfer in blank, it is your
duty to see the transfer is completed by personally in-
specting the share-register. Otherwise, your liability
still exists. Mere enquiry at the office is insufficient. In
Weedon v. City and County Bank, plaintiff had inquired
at the bank, and been told his name was not on the
register. This was untrue, and his executors have to
pay.
* * * *
A Bill, providing that one having trust-money
should pay that into a bank to trust account, and file
with the Registrar account of the same with verifying
affidavit, every three months, under severe penalties,
would meet a crying evil. It should apply to all, and
hold out reward to informers. A law is more effective
when fear of an interested informer exists.

Bingara (N.S.W)) has at last succeeded in
attracting the attention of some bloated capitalist, and
things are beginning to hum : —“Tenders wanted for the
supply (purchase) of om horse to work puddling machine
at the Diamond Mines, Bingara; also for the supply and
delivery of 50 sheets or more of bark.” Envious pro-
prietors of other shows will notice that the co. doesn t
merely want the loan of a moke, it proposes to buy
him, lock, stock and barrel. Apparently it will only run
to hiring the bark, “purchase” being carefully omitted.
Rumored that Captain Monte Cristo will shortly call for
tenders for a long-handled shovel (purchase).
The Junction, at the deep (675 ft.) level, is open-
ing u-p quite satisfactorily ; ana the Junction North more
than satisfactorily. The latter pays a div. this month,
though only a sixpenny. The mine has taken a good deal
of straightening up. Lately the shaft has been pulled to
pieces ; it was about the worst-arranged on the Barrier.

Central North had a flutter, the other day. The
ground was “ a little more ‘heady,’ and showed indica-
tions of a change of strata.” And up shares bounced —
to fall again. But the incident was significant. It won’t
take the actual striking of a lode to send these shares up
rocket-high.

Five hundred men are building the Cockle
Creek sulphide works (Ashcroft’s process), and the first

* * * *
What was referred to some time back in The
Bulletin as the Junction South is now being prospected
by the Yancowinna Co., an Adelaide.concern, and big
things may be developed. For instance, it may prove
that the B.H. Proprietary has so - far' not touched one of
its mineral blocks. This claim (hereafter in this place to
be called the Junction South, since it wa®.only called the
other thing because it failed to float as the J.S.) is peg
and peg south of the Junction, with the British
on the east. Now, the Broken Hill lode has
a pretty true course from the South to Block
14 ; but the big Junction deposit is a quarter
of a mile at least out of line. The question is
whether the connection between the Junction and the
rest of the lode is wholly through the British, or whether
there is a branch running out from the R.H. Proprietary
(further south, that is) to the Junction. If the latter,
the Junction South must have it, and so must one of the
water-blocks of the Proprietary. The writer doesn’t
know. On one hand, the Junction South people are un-
doubtedly opening out on a lode-formation, which carries
silicious ironstone containing chlorides as well as carbon-
ate of lead ; but, on the other hand, the Brisbane Blocks
Co. (long defunct) put down a bore in this same neighbor-
hood, but a little further south, and, though nearly 2000 ft.
was pierced, got no sign of the underlay.
* * * *
Re coal-mines ventilation. One of the principal
N.S.W. colleries, employing upwards of 200 men and
boys, has its mouth situated on a hill-side, and half-a-
mile further up is sunk the ventilating shaft in a gap in
the mountains. At ordinary times the air is well Up to
the mark, but when a westerly wind blows the whole
force of the wind comes through their funnel-shaped gap
and down the shaft. Should the fire be low, or the
furnace-man absent, the air is turned , and, escaping
through old workings and defective trap-doors, fails
altogether to ventilate the mine. What little does get to
the miners is loaded with sulphurous fumes and smoke and
the deadly black-damp of the disused workings. Day
after day is the air thus turned, writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent. When the fire is in good working order and
well-cleaned the state of affairs is worse, for there is a
fight between the upcast draught and the air outside, and
every fresh gust nearly turns back the flames. The flame
from an ordinary pit-lamp will stand as straight as in a
room, and perhaps for several hours hardly 1000 feet a
minute will pass where between 20,000 and 30,000 are
wanted. The effect on the miners, wheelers and horses
is terrible.

Boomster Alick Forrest has withdrawn his
attempt to reduce the labor-conditions on W.A. gold
leases. The opulent £20,000-a-year mining-engineer,
George Grey, has intimated his intention of abandoning a
large area in Lake Lefroy district, on the ground that the
labor-conditions are altogether excessive for purely ex-
ploring and prospecting work. True, the regulations do
require amendment in this direction.

* * * •
The shareholders in the Plunderderry, at a meet-
ing held in London July 22nd, decided to devote £15,000
of the £30,000 working capital still remaining to the pur-
chase of outside properties; to abandon all the outside
blocks, except the West Londonderry, or so much of it as
would bring the area of the original claim up to 25a., and
to continue sinking and driving in the main block. Lord
Fingall and Mr. Myring said the threatened litigation of
the finders prevented them from immediately putting
their shares of the flotation profits into an exploration co.
for the benefit of shareholders. They had no doubt that
it was the intention of the late Col. North to give up his
profits, but his executors considered that they could not
do it without an order of the Court. Seeing that about
£750,000 in cash and shares was given for the London-
derry, what sort of outside properties do they expect to
buy with £15,000 ?

Gold found at Surrey Hills, within seven miles

* * * *
Rontgen rays have been put to a new use at
Ballarat School of Mines, where F. J. Martell and W.
Huey Steele have applied them to chunks of quartz. The
stone is transparent and the metal opaque, so an approxi-
mate estimate of true value can be obtained which puts a
Westralian expert to shame every time.

* * * *
Regulation of oyster-beds by law in Torres Straits
is a failure. The law forbids the removal of shell under
6in., but this regulation is evaded by opening all small
shells taken and removing pearls (if any) and throwing
the young oysters back into the sea—which means their
destruction. A special inspector would be needed for
every boat almost to check this practice. The yield is
steadily decreasing. In 1891 the average yield per boat
was £625 ; in 1895 only £391.
Govt.-resident Douglas has no faith in State
regulation preventing casualties in the diving business.
According to the same authority, fatalities often take
place in comparatively shallow water —so law has hitherto
been based on a false idea. The passion for more
shell is very strong in most divers, and defies all attempts
to check it. An old diver went down this year with fatal
results. As he lay dying he remarked pathetically, “ I’m
sorry I’m going. The water was clear, and I could have
got plenty more shell."

* * • •
He the assay-puff newspaper fraud. In selecting
a piece of stone for assay a man never takes a poor-look-
ing piece. He always prefers a piece showing gold. You
see a man carefully hunt over a 30-ton paddock of stone
for an hour for a suitable piece to assay. What is the
value of that assay as to the quality of the paddock ? A
thousand tons put through the battery is a good, prac-
tical assay; 2000 tons is still better. When will people
cease to be fooled by assays?

To The Bulletin from America by a mining
man not unknown in Australia: —“ I am very busy
looking up mines, and hope soon to get what I want.
America is having a mining boom, and it looks as though
it had come to stay. We are also having a silver boom,
or, rather, a great - silver talk, for I don’t think it will ever
amount to anything else. Some London co’s have already
done big legitimate business here, and their representa-
tives are travelling all over the fields on the look-out for
mines.”
Mr. R. A. Fraser, inventor of the Fraser
railway automatic door-controller, writes :
Dear Bulletin,—A paragraph in your issue of 29th ult.
re the Eraser railway automatic door-controller is calcu-
lated to convey so misleading an impression that I am
constrained to ask for a correction. The very first prin-
ciple of the Fraser controller is that it renders accident
to life or limb practically impossible; in fact, it is de-
signed to protect the public against the consequences of
their own blindness and stupidity. This, and the reduc-
tion of working-expenses, are the chief points claimed for
the invention. The doors are not “ banged all at once by
an invisible power” ; the doors could not possibly jamb
passengers’ arms or heads, as suggested, because the very
faintest resistance on their part would suffice to hold the
door pending the removal of the obstacle, after which the
door would close itself. All the doors are subject to the
controller, yet each door can be manipulated independ-
ently of the controller, and separately. Lastly, Commis-
sioner Mathieson did not decline the patent on account of
any defect which it possessed; the defect, as lam led to
believe, lay in the coffers of the Railway Department;
and, anyhow, I didn’t press it on him. I prefer, in fact,
to dispose of the appliance in London.
In Memoriam.
JULIAN THOMAS, “The Vagabond.”
“ The Vagabond is dead ! ” What mem’ries
throng
Around his name ! Men miss the master hand
That limned such living pictures of our land
And distant seas and shores ; the spirit strong
To urge the Right and reprobate the Wrong ;
The sympathy that seemed to understand
Unspoken sorrows; and the mind that planned
Great schemes that to the future still belong.
And women knew him chivalrous and true ;
And children loved to gather round his knee;
None ever sought him in the hour of need
In vain, if he could aid : What he could do
He did in secret, not that men might see ;
And cynic speech oft hid benignant deed !
“ He died in harness ! ” So he ivished should close
His life of arduous work. The facile pen,
That, to the last, could charm the minds of men
Just laid aside, when Nature gave repose
To him who loved her, him whose virile prose
Rang out with more than poet’s fervor when
He hymned her praises—surely now and then
She doth to such her mysteries disclose !
Wanderer, thy many journeyings are o’er ;
Writer, thy last fair “ copy ” has been writ !
Friend of the poor, widow and orphan mourn
That they shall know thy sympathy no more,
And tears and prayers, like floral emblems knit
On love and sorrow, shall thy tomb adorn.
A.V.G.

The apotheosis of advertisement—a suicide club.
Judge A’Becket held, the other day, in Vic-
toria, that the mere fact of a husband visiting a
bagnio, even though it were “only to shout for the
girls, you know, ” is conduct which the court will
hold as conducing to a wife’s subsequent adultery,
and accordingly a sufficient ground to justify the
court in refusing the husband a divorce for such
adultery. One phrase used by the Judge, “mis-
conduct which might provoke her to retaliatory
infidelity,’’supplies a long-felt want in legal ter-
minology. What a long act of the Comedie
Humaine might not a patient Balzac cram under
such a title as “ retaliatory infidelity ? ”
Hubby, when he got a decree nisi, fondly
imagined he was financially clear of his bad bar-
gain. But, as was proved in a recent Vic. case,
hubby is liable for maintenance pending the order
absolute. Which opens up the possibility of a
smart woman running up a big score during the
intervening stage for necessaries (?), for hubby to
pay or fight over.
* * * *
A pretty Vic. heiress, whose money was
made out of unromantic beer, is at present re-
ported to be dying, partly of an old-fashioned
disease called love, and, in a degree, through her
efforts to exist upon two mouthfuls of food per
diem, with no liquid to wash it down.
* * * *
Missionary Lawes, in defence and applause
of Chinese and other missions, says that “ the loss
of life in the mission-field is a mere nothing to the
sacrifice of life on the hunting-field and race-
course ; yet we hear nothing about discountenanc-
ing or stopping either form of sport.” The worst
of all parsons is that they won’t box according to
Queensberry, or any other, rules. It appears to be
impossible for a parson to state a parallel case. In
the first place, no one wants to discountenance or
stop males who want to be missionaries, from
going to China to be disembowelled, or to New
< Juinea to be baked ; it is the female whom white
men would preserve from horrible chances. How
many accidents to females are there on the race-
course ? About one in a century. Again, let Mr.
Lawes stand on his own contrast, even. The
females at hunting-meets (taking the other in-
stance he gave) are about 1000 to 1 of the females
missioning in savage lands. Will the New Guinea
missionary assert that the proportion of females
(or of males and females combined) killed in hunt-
ing-fields is anything like the number of females
killed, or maimed, or ravished, in mission-fields
among savages ?

Before a N.S.W. country Bench, a few
days since, a youth of 17 years was charged by
his father with assault. Witnesses fully proved
the case, and showed that the boy was ah absolute
young ruffiail. He. would have got off with a
small fine, but he set up the defence that as he was
. in the eyes of the law an infant it was the duty of
rnu * a , , er k ee P him till he was of legal age.
xhe old man, he said, would not accept this very
reasonable view—lienee he had proceeded to bash
it into him with a club. The young man’s time
for the next two months will mainly be occupied
in searching for the soft side of the plank he tries
to sleep on.

The earnings of the average typewriter are
AT THE H.S.W. SOCIETY OF ARTISTS' SHOW.
at the best precarious. The usual rate of payment
is 2d. per folio (72 words) for a first copy, and Id.
per folio for extra carbon copies. If there were
plenty of jobs a typist could make a level pound
daily ; but, between scarcity of work and “bad
Pays,” the key-pounder has but a hand-to-mouth
existence. It is now seriously proposed to reduce
the rate to Id. per folio and ?>d. for carbon extras,
and doubtless tire soulless individuals who give
their work out will even sweat the machinist below
this pitiful rate. Some idea of the pickings made
by judges’ associates and others, who are allowed
Is. and over per folio, and have the copying type-
written, may thus be imagined. Only the other
week tenders were called in Sydney for making
eight copies of an 88-folio document, and the work
was gladly done by a hard-up widow typist for
15s. —£3 would only have been moderate remune-
ration.

* . * * A- *
“ Cold pig” is one objection to the Satur-
day half-holiday with many shopkeepers. This
is a term of art used to describe the customer who
returns next day to change the article purchased.
If she buys on Saturday, she usually wears it on
Sunday, and so foregoes her rights. But if she
buys on Friday night, they say, but tell it not in
Gath, Saturday morning will be mainly occupied
with her and sister “ cold pigs.”

* * * *
It all depends on the kind of shop your
father kept. There seems to be an unwritten law
that, while “ you may be as genteel as never was
and brew, yet you cannot be genteel and bake.”
Aspiring drapers rush into the upper air like a
gas-balloon escaped from the hand of a child ;
other trades seem lead-weighted. The infallible
plan used to be to make your pile, go to Eng-
land and hire some stony Hon. Mrs. or Lady to
present your feminines at Court, and, after being
touched by Royalty for the Evil of Comnroness,
all taint of the shop vanished, and the family
spread themselves to the smiles of Toorak or Potts
Point. Now, Royalty seems to have lost its value,
or something. There are several families who
have gone through the whole programme cor-
rectly, have returned per P. and 0., duly broken
off the two youngest girls’ engagements with the
fifth and sixth officers, acquired the latest London
accent, French hats, and contempt for Australia,
and yet they are still standing off and on waiting
A WILD CAT ITEM.
Why the rabbit-trouble is not yet very bad in Westralia.
for a favoring breeze to waft them in among the
Nicest People. Court presentations don’t do the
trick nowadays, evidently.

The oldest N.S.W. lady State-school
teacher died last week. Deceased lady served for
over 40 years, and had contributed to the Super-
annuation Pund from its inception to the day of
her death ; but, because she didn’t retire from the
fund when the chance offered recently, and didn’t
live to reach the pension-age (60), her family can’t
claim and won’t get a penny of the large amount
which sne was—up to very recently —forced to
contribute to the wMd-cat fund.

* * * *
The latest Melb. cycling church-parade
exhibited a new development of the feminine
scorcher: a young, pretty, and shapely cycliste
appearing with her corsetless form encased in
grey tweed knickers, an open jacket to match, and
a low-collared, man-like, white woollen sweater.
She was dressed for pace, and she kept faith with
her attire. Also, her presence was a great help to
male devotion.
* * * *
Prominent amongst the dancers at Melb.
Mayor Storey’s ball was the young lady-doctor
who headed the honor-list in her final year, and in
consequence possesses the coveted privilege of
galvanising “drunks” and sewing-up “ accidents”
in Melb. Hospital casualty-ward. Unlike most
clever girls, Mdlle. Sawbones is as frisky, pretty,
saucy, and well-grown as if she hardly knew a
black pill from a chocolate-cream. Partners,
patients, and grudging he-surgeons all sooner or
later fall under the spell of this charming anomaly,
whose professional future would look rose-colored
indeed only for that rumored rock ahead —matri-
mony.

This tendency to speculate about the possible
address of the deceased is ruining the “ In Memo-
riam ” business.
* * * *
The libel-verdict for £5O and costs given
last week against the Australian Star apparently
establishes the principle that a reporter cannot
take a charge against a person from the police-
sheet or information, but to be safe must report it
as it is actually read out in court. At present
about 75 per cent, of police-court proceedings in
the dailies are merely copied from the “papers ”
in each ease.
* * * »
About the most horrible yarn this year is
that of the stowaway on the s.s, Namoi. Presum-
ably he was drunk, or else he was wholly ignorant
of the possibilities of the case, for he seems to have
deliberately lowered himself into a hopeless cul-de-
sac among the boilers, and, when steam was got
up, he slowly fried and sizzled in his prison. His
last effort, apparently, was to climb out by shinning
up a steam-pipe, itself as hot as Nebuchadnezzar’s
furnace. The only case at all resembling it which
The Bulletin remembers is that of a stowaway
who saw a chance to hide himself inside the big
boiler; a plate had been removed, and he had no
idea what a boiler was for, so he took advantage
of the chance. His bones were afterwards dis-
covered, and his boiled hat and boots, but nothing
more.
* * * *
If it is a fact—and Minister Gould, off his
guard in an Assembly debate, appears to have ad-
mitted the truth of the statement —that Judge
Windeyer sought to discount the alibi sworn
to in the Suffield case by asserting, in his
private report to the Executive, that one of the
women who sought to establish it was a mistress
of Suffield’s, the troubles of ex-Justice Mount
Rennie are only just beginning. The husband of
the woman whose character is impugned is a deter-
mined man, with perfect faith in his wife, whose
character is locally considered excellent. Also,
the allegation that Suffield possessed an available
mistress is regarded as by no means calculated to
strengthen the charge of assault with brutal and
protracted violence alleged against the accused.

* * * »
The most recent sensation in Perth (W.A.)
is the discovery of tuberculosis. The Chief In-
spector of Stock, acting “ upon information re-
ceived,” reported the existence of the disease in
several animals in some slaughter-houses near
Perth, and generally stated that the slaughter-
houses were filthy, and that the pigs gathered
around the dead and diseased carcases and feasted
upon the blood and corruption, and varied the
sameness of the courses by devouring aborted
calves, and were themselves wasting away with
disease. The proofs of the existence of the disease
were unmistakable, and, so that they should be
brought home to the Govt., samples of the rotten
matter were bottled and gracefully placed in the
tea-room of the Leg. Assembly. In spite of all
this evidence, the Govt, and its supporters in the
House, who are all more or less interested in the
bullock-trade, seemed to treat the whole affair rather
lightly, and despise the alleged fresh discovery of
SO diseased bullocks; they said that while they
would do all they could to protect the public
health, the existence of the disease had yet to be
proved.
This diseased-meat and milk question is one on
which millions should if necessary be spent by the
State. The community is convulsed when a man
poisons his wife or a wife her husband ; but the
community is yet too ignorant to realise that
the carcase of a single cancerous or tuberculosed
beast may spread ruin and agonising death among
scores and indirectly among hundreds of citizens.
The auctioneer or butcher who knowingly sells
diseased meat is a poisoner and a felon, and should
be treated as such. And the politician in power
who fails in one jot of his duty on this truly
tremendous social question incurs the moral re-
sponsibility attaching to wholesale murder.

to lumpy-jaw (cancer). Ugh !

* * * *
The Q. Minister for Education was re-
quested the other day to take steps to prevent
colored children attending State schools. Some
short time ago the Railway Commissioners were
also petitioned to set aside (on certain lines) a
special carriage for the travelling heathen. Already
in many districts the Kanaka has his separate
church and Sunday-school; for the white
Kerristian, who is to sing hymns and swap harps
with him in the sweet by-and-bye, won’t touch
his colored brethren with a 40ft. pole even on the
blessed Sawbath.

A charitable lady, through a Melb. city
missionary, gave a poor man last week a blanket
and rug for his swag. The poor man was arrested
by the police for having “ property reasonably
supposed to be stolen,” and spent three days in
gaol.

The Presbytery of Sydney described Rev.
George Hay’s statement “ that he had to make a
living for his family ” as very low ground for a
minister of religion to take up, and immediately
after proceeded to amalgamate various congrega-
tions—so that, combined, they would be better
able to support their parsons than they now can
singly. Which latter seems to be Mr. Hay’s piece
of ground with a coat of paint on the fence.
* * * *
Melbourne is spreading itself for a luscious
breach-of-promise case, in which plaintiff is a
nurse and defendant a battered fragment of over
70, who was helped through a recent illness by the
lady. From nurse to nursery is a short shift.
What does the bad old duke in “ The Notorioiis
Mrs. Ebbsmith ” say about the nurses with their
black dresses and snowy bibs ? No single man
should ever place himself in the hands of a nurse
without first obtaining from her a formal legal
document to the effect that she will not hold him
responsible for any proposals he may make whilst
under her hands.

Bread-and-butter weddings are coming
into vogue in Yic. Two people recently dis-
tinguished themselves, firstly, by having a full
account of their wedding published a week before
it took place, and, nextly, by giving a tea-and-
coffee strict-temperanee “ swarry ” at a swell pub.
after the ceremony. The stern, solemn visages of
tbe guests on leaving the festive board reminded
the bystanders of a body of shareholders in the
City of Melb. Bank.
**» - *
In Melbourne the other day a man went to
drown a dog in the Yarra. He went too.near, or
else took too violent a heave at the dog, and they
went in together. The dog promptly got out ; the
man was fished out with a boat hook two hours
later, and when his remains got home the dog was
already there drying himself before the fire.

A Bulletin correspondent remarks, for
the benefit of the N.S.W. Minister of Justice,
that it is impolitic to allow colored men to execute
distress warrants. However, it’s not very likely
that much notice will be taken of such a small
complaint in a country where a noseless hangman
is among the tortures of the condemned.

* * * *
A Hawthorn, Vie., publican was fined the
other day for selling better whisky than the brand
on the bottle indicated. It was a technical crime,
and it is hoped the lesson will not be lost on the
other publican guilty of the same offence—if he
still lives.
* • * *
A most flourishing industry in W.
Queensland is the vending of opium-charcoal to
aboriginals. There are Chows who have paid the
best part of £lOO in fines for the offence within a
year. The other day Ah Pow faced Clermont
bench and pleaded in palliation of his sin that it
was not genuine opium he had sold, but simply an
after-product. “ Chinaman smoke opium self,
then Sellee charcoal longa nigger. Allee same, my
' countlyman use’m tea first, then sell old leaf ’longa
Eulopean.” The truth of this latter statement is
probably beyond cavil, but it’s hard to get it
smacked in our faces like this by a pig-tailed
heathen.
A COOLGARDIE NOTE: INFRA DIG.
When making next-of-kin responsible for
the keep of indigent persons in Govt, institutions,
N.S.W. authorities should take occasion to coerce
in similar fashion employers who send sick ser-
vants to subsidised hospitals and infirmaries, to be
treated at public expense. Quite recently, a
highly-paid State official thus devolved on the
public the care and attendance of a servant girl,
and, on being remonstrated with, displayed
tremendous indignation.
* * * *
The latest case on a bulky Sydney list of
collectors for bogus charities, and bogus collectors
for real charities, serves to show how long the
swindle can be successfully worked. The other
day a man of many aliases got six months for
obtaining £1 by false pretences for “the Charity
Aid Society ” —which was also another name for
himself. He had been in the bogus-charity busi-
ness for three years, and had made over
say a steady £2 10s. per week. One of his dodges
was to get each subscriber in turn to initial his
subscription, and thus infuse confidence into the
next victim.

Shot in the 22nd parallel a 16ft. carpet
snake covered with ticks (dozen to square inch).
Deceased was moving South rapidly with his
cargo and had neither been dipped, scoured, nor
scarified —in direct contravention of regulations.

A N.S.W. up-country P.M., in hearing a
case wherein a man was accused of disturbing the
place by ringing a ferry-bell in the middle of the
night, settled the matter in characteristic fashion.
The defence was that accused, desiring to cross the
river in the night, only rang the bell often enough
to waken the ferryman. “ That won’t stand,”
vociferated the “binch” —“you wakened me up
with your awful row and I’m going to fine you.
Which he did.
CAUGHT.
I met a charming little Sin
Three nights agone ;
To share my soul, a saint-like shape
Sweet Sin put on.
She cast her meshes over me
In filmy fold,
Soft as a spider gossamer
Of fined gold.
And now for sake of one bright Sin,
So sweet and small,
My soul its liberty has lost
Beyond recall.
Lost without season to repent—
All hope is past;
I could not free me as I would—
Sin holds me fast!
Sydney.

At Great Northern, near Rutherglen, Vic.,
was slaughtered, the other day, an ox which, when
skinned, was found to have 14 ribs on each side
being two more than cattle usually have. The
bullock was 2| years old and scaled 7901b5.
A N.S.W. railway-watchman caught a
humorous Chinaman going off with a load of iron
lutings. John explained to the magistrate that
as it was a cold night he was merely carrying
the iron to keepi himself warm.

Hie Council of the Journalists’ lnstitute
met in Melbourne the other day after a long inter-
val of silence, and bewailed the want of interest
taken in. the dying concern. After which the
authorities solemnly recommended medicine as
before—the wiping off of all subscription-arrears
m the hope of galvanising their ill-fated bantling
into fresh existence. One member varied the pro-
ceedings by declaring that the Institute had been
run entirely in the interests of proprietors This
was an exaggerated statement, however. The
Institute may have crawled, or toddled, in the
interests of J>. Syme and others, but it can’t be
said to have run at any time during its rickettv
career. J
Julian Thomas, “The Vagabond,” is dead
at about 60 years, after a tempestuous career
Concerning his early days nothing is known for
sure ; but he is believed to have been a Virginian
and to have fought in the War of Secession. Cer-
tain it is that when he first reached Melbourne
say about 20 years ago, he was a man who had
seen many countries ; to the wide range of lands
and men over which he incidentally gossiped was
cue much of the charm of his memorable first con-
tributions to the Argus. “ The Vag. ” was essen-
ohemian ’ £ crie -rous and reckless ; he died
a bachelor Bohemian’s death in lonely lodgings
and, though long in a hopeless state of health’
maintained himself to the last by his pen. The
slenderly-remunerated task-work of his latter days
was awful advertising grubbery for such a man.
In recording the death of this brave-hearted cos-
mopolitan the “ big ” Melbourne papers illustrate
their littleness of spirit. The Argus gives you to
understand his career was ended when lie left
that shop, and chops short off the most fruitful
and useful, though, to be sure, not the most
dazzling years of his life. The Age is more
sensible, and notes his Argus deeds, carefully
abstaining from mention of the Argus. How either
paper would have shone by a generous touch !

Unartistic “artists”— and Others: A Yell.
The notable thing about the second Exhibition
o f the N.S.W. Society of artists ’.is that
i isn t altogether an exhibition of Art.
t has 1(>9 objects in frames and out of
lern—mostly oleographs, photographs, daubs,
prettinesses and uninterestingnesses — which
aren t Art. Premier Reid is there: he is pink
and piggy—-but he isn’t Art. There is no title to
his alleged portrait, and no name would suit it but
Georgey Porgey Puddeney Pie. Little Eva is
" * e ’ dying in the limelight, with her agonised
mother or aulit or grandmother praying by—but
neither Little Eva nor her weary old grand-
mother is Art. There is a flaccid girl in a
greenery-yallery gown gazing over a greenery-
iluery sea—-she isn’t Art either. There are light-
hlue splotches with dark-blue splotchy variations ;
and big red dabs sprinkled with a lot of little
yellow dabs ; and rainbows chopped up into small
pieces and stuck on a canvasall which are
mgvdy curious and amusing, but certainly are not
* * • »
It is a lot easier to say what isn’t Art than to
say what Art is. Zangwill, who is a neat and
acute critic, calls Art “ an Artist’s reaction to
the stimulus of the universe.” Which is
acute and neat—and begs the question by
kindly passing the difficulty to the Artist. What
is an Artist. 1 ' Obviously, an Artist is a person
whose reaction to the stimulus of the,; uni verse is
Art—and there you are back in the old hole again !
i he Bulletin’s definition of Art is “Man’s
idealisation of Nature’’-which it likes a
ittle better because specifying a little more
the character and quality of the Artist’s
leaction, while it candidly admits that the
hole and you are still in statu. You oan
! ,n b R e t out by staying in — bv assum-
ing that according to the current standard of taste
of more-or-less intelligent people Art should be
true, or it should be beautiful, or it should be
moving, or stimulating, or suggestive, or all com-
bined ; and whatever it be, it should represent
not merely the thing that is, but the thing that
plight be. Art, that is, should not merely copy
i ature, but should refine and spiritualise it,
elevate it-as an Artist can. So that here comes
another defimtion-Art is Nature raised to the
Alanth power—Nature glorified and raised to her
hifthest expression by the spark of divinity in the
human mind.
* * * *
This reads nice and obscure and metaphysical
(as is surely appropriate), and it is to be hoped
brings the reader a little nearer comprehension of
the point desired to be reached-which is that the
Society of artists, as a body, don’t elevate their
themes. And they mast, according to The Bul-
letin 8 assumption, in order to be Artists There
seems to be an impression in “artistic” circles
that Art is mechanical dexterity— correct draw-
mg good coloring nice composition, and so on.
iNot a bit of it : these things are only the clothes
hung on the dressmaker’s dummy, the “ Art ”
is the pretty woman who wears them. They are
dead ; they appeal to the eye with a certain deco-
iati\ e value; but the essence of Art is its appeal
18 w hy Longstaff’s “Lady in
f . 1S0n e of the most Artistic pictures in the
exhibition The “lady” is like a real, living
woman : she smiles at you, claims your attention •
you want_i o know her and have her with you' '
bho is inevitable,” too-right just as she is
color and dress and all-you would not have her
otherwise: the little bunch of violets is just what
she ought to carry-the red flower which the
average artist ” would have put in her hand
would spoil her. And it is not sex-appeal which
produces the effect: it is the artist’s handling of
his theme To be sure, technical dexterity is ne-
cessary to him, but it plays a small part compara-
tively in the Art of the result.
* * * *
Longstaff gets an advantage from his size,
of course : a good big picture is more impressive
than a good little picture. There is a charm
Lh?” ed t? - hlS ? J V A \ ? ulIer ’ s “Tambourine
UirJ. It is natural and human and true • the
eyes ha,ve a soul behind them. Most of the por-
traits in the exhibition have no souls behind
their eyes: there are old gentlemen and old
laches—some well-painted, some ill-but hardly
a ?7 °* i , lem ave real Artistic worth. One
old gentleman, as presented, has precisely the
same mental value as another old gentleman-i.e.
no value at all. Exceptions are Julian Ashton’s
Bush Poet —who has really some thought and
suggestuon in him, and is cleverly painted to
boot, and Tom Roberts fine, strong, “round”
portrait of a self-reliant-looking old squatter.
Clever is generally the word for Julian Ash-
ton ; but you can t do anything Artistically with
some of his pictures. “ The Smiling Maid ’’might
be one of two just now hanging to a drunken sailor
opposite The Bulletin office, and that is all the
use oi her.
* * * ,
“ TL L ?rr £ 300 for his ambitious picture,
1 he Death of Burke. Its ambition is a good
point about it: The Bulletin wants Australian
artists to try and handle grand themes grandly
instead of pettifogging over flowc'r-panels and
pot-boilers; but there isn’t -£300 wortti of art in
The Death of Burke.” It isn’t “ The Death of
Burke. Doubtless, Burke’s death was utterly
unlike anything that A. Loureiro or anybody
else can conceive; but the present writer
complains because the artist hasn’t realised its
conception (which is arbitrary and dogmatic, of
course; but a critic in the last resort can be
nothing else but an arbitrary dogmatist :
ins relative value depends entirely on the
capacity with which he expresses tlie ideas of
other arbitrary dogmatists of a certain relative
intellectual status ; and if the artist objects—well
his objection is just as arbitrary and dogmatic)!
So it is averred that “The Death of Burke” is
too pink.and poetical; that his billy is too big for
him, and his misery too small; that there are no
points about Burke to distinguish him from
Brown, or Jones, or Robinson ; and though there
are some strength and pathos in the watching figure
(omitting the legs), the whole picture has not the
strength and pathos of Gordon’s word-picture
which you can buy (with much other strength and
pathos given in) for ,£299 17s. 6d. less than Mr
Loureiro s picture would cost you ;-and so
on. Nevertheless, this scribe doesn’t want
to dishearten Mr. Loureiro (is it possible for
Ihe Bulletin to dishearten an “ artist ”’)•
it looked a long time at his picture!
and then it came back and looked again •
and it A. Loureiro to brood a little more
over his conception, and get a little more sand-
waste and desolation into his soul, and sacrifice
pink poetry to tragic force, and come back next
year with a forbidding “Death of Leichhardt ’
which shall smirch your marrow while you are
still mounting the staircase.
* * * *
Alice Muskett shows a number of little canvases
—neat, clean, clever canvases, with little Art in
them so far. In the study of “Violets and Old
Letters,” the violets seem tangled up with the old
letters, and, anyway, there is no poetry in th e
presentation of them. Now, all the poetry of th e
universe can be put into one violet—and, plus one
old letter the universe really isn’t big enough to
contain the crowding thoughts of amourtcndre and
auld lang syne and the days upon the Suwanee
river. We can get something of this poetry into
the Literature of violets and old letters : why can’t
Alice Muskett get it into the Art? Your
■ W 'mi s ar easil y satisfied nowadays. Here
is Hall Thorpe, for example, who has painted a
back view of a girl by moonlight. There is no-
thing in life more attractive than the back view of
a girl by moonlight (her front view generally
spoils it), and there is certainly three guineas’
i, ml suggestion in the picture; but
u ij fhorpe is an Artist (notan “artist”) he
should have exhausted all the possibilities of his
subject instead of merely suggesting one or two of
them. r J he girl should be there : Hall Thorpe has
only given an imperfect 9-inches-by-5 hint of the
girl who might be there. And in the vicinity
are many pictures which, if they claimed to be
Literature, would be regarded as possessing “no
particular novelty or interest.” Some of them
are flat, mechanical copies of Nature—photo-
graphs plus color which often doesn’t im-
prove the photograph; others are crude, raw
impiessions (another name for lazy - man’s
jvork) which help to give the Exhibition its half-
baked look; some, like F. A. FullePs “French
Peasant,” have a certain .historical or sociological
value, as supplying an image of what that particu-
lar kind of creature has been or is ; but none of
them has Art. And what shall it profit an
artist if he gain the whole world of technical
tricks, and lose his own Art—his message his
power to elevate his theme, to heighten the
human ideal ? It is like losing, for the sake of
mere life, the things which make Life worth
living.

Conor, contains no artists above the level of the ordinary
stock companies of the colonial stage. ’
From a Bulletin man’s diary:—“Went to see the
c.farming Mrs. B. Potter—that astonishing wriggle with a
dab of red grease-paint atop-kind of sea-serpent by
sunset. Play School for Scandal”—Sheridan un-
acknowledged in advt. or programmes—apparently for
fear he’d attract some attention from said B.P.—who
impudently murders one of his best effects in order to
get her little jammy line in at the climax. Sheridan
exits Sir Peter with ‘I go—but I leave mv character
behind me ’—and the situation closes naturally and
forcibly. But Mrs. P: grins and minces to get the audi-
ence s attention, wriggles to the back of the set, and
interpolates a nasal anti-climax: ‘ S-hir P-heter, y-hou’d
b-hetter t-hake it w-hitb h-you.’ Ugh! She strikes me
as one of those confounded American prodigy children,
spoilt in their infancy, heads turned in their teens.”
Walter Bentley, after his frequent fiascos, fizzles, and
frosts, has bade adieu to the ghost that walked so un-
■ stably He is now teaching the amateur-actor johnnies
at Brisbane, and holds Govt, appointments in most of the
Schools of Art in Bananaland, which grateful country has
rewarded him with a free railway-pass. Walter does not
intend to open “ shop ” again. When leggy Gov. Larning-
ton made his first entry into Toowoomba the Shakespearian
mummer sat with him in the vice-regal chariot. Walter,
at this rate, will soon be reaching out for a knighthood.
George Rignold, recognising the saving disposition ot
lovely woman, employes Miss Gena Coppin as his trea-
surer and secretary. Miss Gena has a keen Coppini tor
the saxpences.
There are now three dramatic co.’s catering for Smell-
bournian suburbs. The “Commonwealth” system is in
vogue with them all, and the class of play is a shrieking,
howling, blood-soaked melodrama, cribbed and gathered
from every sensational production since Noah “ pi,ed up ”
ins mud-barge on the heights of Ararat.
Lawton the Boneman, in “ Chinatown,” is a reminder
of Billy West, an Australian variety-artist of a while ago.
Ail he had was a bone solo, but it carried him round the
gl >be.
Inasmuch as Lady Irving attended the Irving Baird
marriage, it goes sans saying that the knight and Ellen
Terry didn’t.
A strolling Melbourne musician boasts that he and mate
have cleared £l2OO in the last two years.
“ Trilby ” Crane has a sister whose voice is to paralyse
the world one day. Meanwhile, she is being iiiuo.oal.y
educated in ’Murka at Trilby’s expense.
An old Bulletin file has this verse on one of Mrs
Potter’s first Australian appearances :
“ A stagey sticking-out of arms,
A frequent faint, a feeble totter,
A voice which rather bores than charms,
A handsome gown -that’s Mrs. Potter.
Though seeming less the more she’s seen,
This compliment ’tis fair to pay her—
When Mrs. Potter plays Pauline
She is indeed a poor lean player.”
Mrs. B.P. doesn’t seem to have changed much in the last
half-dozen years. Neither, so far as she is concerned, does
Tiie Bulletin.

Personal Items.
Cardinal Moran will be 66 on Wednesday,
Sept. 16.
Agar Wynne may be Victoria’s next
Agent-General.
L. L. Smith has placed his huge foot in
W.A., where many young men don’t feel very
well just now.
“Jack” Want enjoys the unique distinc-
tion of having refused a Premiership and a
Supreme Court Judgeship before passing middle
age.
N.S.W. has never had a teetotal Premier,
and, according to a prominent politician, stands
no show of having one till the Labor-party gets
into power.
St. James’s Budget, by special permission,
gives a Rontgen of the Prince of Wales’ hand.
Honor Science ! The full hand—that of Baccarat
and Tranby Croft.
Victorian : “ Premier Turner is like the
man in Balzac’s ‘ Peau de Chagrin,’giving away
his life for his work. Do not let our Treasurer
become a Ballance. ”
Vite-veskited (Hebrew) Zox, M.P. (in
Vic. Assembly): “Some members have scruples
against wine. * Now, my antipathy is swine. But
I don’t compel others,” &c.
Be talk of James Smith seceding to the
Age. If it happens, then Marcus Clarke, Julian
Thomas, and James Smith will represent a re-
markable trio of Argus deserters.
People surmised that pastor Fitchett must
have penned “Deeds that Won the Empire,” in
the Argus, because they were so cracked up by
all the parsons—except the Wesleyan.
At a N.S.W. inland town, the other day,
there were six J.’sP. on the bench. The three
men reporters were all J.’sP., the C.P.S. was a
J.P., and so was every attorney at the table.
The proposal to memorialise Samuel
Marsden reminds old Sydneyites that the rev. ex-
blacksmith’s daughter became the wife of the
original Anthony Hordern. She visited England,
and died at sea when returning to Australia.
Aide-de-camp Lord Shaftesbury is not half
bad as an amateur actor. He has a trained tenor
voice, and a pretty good stage face, which isn’t
spoilt even by his aggressive teeth. Shaftesbury’s
celebrated grandfather is noted for his connection
with the Factory Acts ; the present Earl is more
likely to be celebrated for his Actory Facts.
The historical
Arthur O’Connor,
grandfather of the
present able, influ-
ential and ortho-
dox “Dick” O’-
Connor, of N.S.
Wales Bar, was,
in his day, the
leading Frenchi-
fied infidel of the
Irish revolution-
ary party. The
party consisted of
many religions,
and all of them
found Arthur a
thorn in their
sides.
N.S.W. Su-
preme Court bro-
thers Darley and
Stephen maintain
angrily - opposing
views on the Kil-
burn Sisters or-
ganisations. The
Woman again !
“ She shall divide
brother and bro-
ther,” prophesies
Swinburne. But
who ever saw two
N.S.W. Supreme

walking down-

Alex. Suther-
land, author of a
book of poems and
several school text
books, has long
been engaged up-
on a big philoso-
phical book now
almost ready to
hurl at the head
of the Cosmos.
The argument is
that physical caus-
ation is sufficient
to have produced
everythingthat is.
Sutherland, from
his picturesque
home under the
shadow of Mount
Martha, lays his
finger on his nose,
winks at the set-
ting sun, jeers at
the rising moon,
and puts out his
tongue at the
ocean, as he says
—“ Gahn ! I’ve
found you all
out.”
Vic. Public Service Board Morrah was
Gov. Latrobe’s first appointee to the local court
service. He is 63 years old, and like Howitt,
chairman of the board, who is 66, is youpg and fit
enough to rule and direct a service that men over
60 years old are not allowed to work in.
Bishop Cowie, of Maoriland, appears to
relieve the monotony of the Worrd with a little
gentle speculation. His carriage hovers sus-
piciously in front of the Stock Exchange, but as
he still goes on preaching against the gambling
sin, the prelate probably sticks to “ safe invest-
ments.”
Mooted in influential circles in Sydney
last week to testimonialise Dr. Jameson, the
Transvaal swashbuckler, and invite him, if he
desires to quit England, to take up his abode in
an Australian capital.
The glamor of colossal architectural
designs intoxicates N.S.W. Premiers. Parkes
adored his deadhouse, Dibbs his hospital, and
Reid wants to worship a palatial Parliament
House-cwm-exhibition.
There died in Adelaide Aug. 26 Shadrach
Ash, aged 96. He arrived in S.A. by the Lady
Mary Pelham (the second vessel to drop anchor in
local waters) 30th July, 1836, and was a newspaper-
runner up to his ninetieth year.
N.S.W. Protectionist leader Lyne pro-
fesses his willingness to meet Max Hirsch in
public fiscal debate. Lyne had better haul off.
The only Australian Protectionist ready enough
to take the floor against the experienced Hirsch is
Trenwith.
Mines-Minister Foster is about the most
oily and polite member of Vic. Government. He
cultivated his party manners in days of old, when
he ran a general store in Omeo, Gippsland, and sup-
plied the local belles with new spring bonnets
and butter.
Obit, aged 77 years, the old Penrith
(N.S.W.) identity, Thomas Smith, of the “Old
Red Gow ” inn, a hostelry well-known in the ante-
railway days. He was the father of Minister for
Mines Sydney Smith and “Tom ” Smith,” M.P.
for the Nepean.
“ There is a tide, ” &c. Had millionaire
Burdekin stuck to Parkes he would have got the
bauble for which he panted. The English authori-
ties are quite ready to distribute a few baronetcies
here and there among reputable Australian citi-
zens able to stand the racket.
The Clarence river (N.S.W.) has some
queer J’sP. One of them recently remarked from
the Bench that the conduct of a witness in a
certain case had been “a perfect enema to him.”
This is nearly as good as the remark of a notorious
personage employed in the Dean case : “ Counsel
on the other side may abuse me as much as he
likes : I don’t care for his epitaphs.”
The Launceston (Tas.) Telegraph - Stra-
chan 1110,000 libel - case has practically been
declared off. The paper has withdrawn the plea
of justification, and apologises in mitigation of
damages. The alleged libel was contained in an
article entitled “ A Modern Priapus. ” As plaintiff
marched all the way from Singapore to take
action, the affair will anyhow cost the paper much
money.
AN OLD-TIME ATHLETE.
This gentleman was, in the brave days of old, “a terrible goal-sneak.” Even
now he attends nearly every football match and barracks as loudly as
fatty degeneration will permit.
Sydney D.T. is never weary of reiterating
that Windeyer, ex-J., “is a man of particularly
strong force of character.” Which, without im-
plying any disrespect to his ex-Honor, might be
said of some of the very worst and most dangerous
criminals who have come before him.
Report has it that ex-Judge Windeyer is
also among the poets and has a little thing of his
own under the impartial eyes of “ home ” critics.
When a young man, dabbling in press work, he
wrung the withers of a halting muse with efforts
which Parkes then commended and used to be
prophetic about.
Thread-the-needle Peacock is, thusfar, one
of the few Vic. politicians unspoilt by success —he
still drinks discreetly and laughs immoderately
with the pals who knew him as a struggling
schoolmaster.
Is it a joke—or what ? On the facade, now
being built, of N.S.W. National Art Gallery are
inscribed, in large block - letters, “ Titian,”
“Giotto,” “Rubens,”and so on; but not one of
the great artists named is represented in the gal-
lery. Another freak of the immortal genius re-
sponsible for the Sydney Post-Office carvings ?
HAD MENDED HIS WAYS.
“ When the Sydney police raided Wong Cow’s
opium den yesterday they found hawkers’ baskets
of vegetables in every sleeping apartment.”
Daily paper.
MRS. FOGARTY: “ An ’ is it after slapin’ wid yer
cabbages ye are, y'ould yellow skin?”
JOHN WUN LUNG: “No, missee, me mallied —
now.”
The right man for the seat on N.S. Wales
Supreme Court Bench vacated by Judge Win-
deyer was, of course, ex-Speaker Barton, Q-p- 5
but then he happened to be of the wrong political
party. Mr. Cohen, the appointee, is a good man
so far as he goes, but he doesn’t go far enough. He
is of District Court stamp only, and his strict res-
Sectability and many admirable traits of character
on’t atone for his want of calibre. Mr. Cohen’s
appointment, while conciliating his powerful clan,
will emphasise the deplorable weakness of a bench
on which, so far as The Bulletin knows, there
does not sit a single man who ever said a wise
thing that wasn’t a platitude or a witty thing that
wasn’t a hideous pun. The present tendency of
N.S. W. Supreme Court Bench is to consist mainly
of pugs and Italian greyhounds instead of mastiffs.
Judge Windeyer’s official gallop has appar-
ently ended in a cul-de-sac —he has bruised himself
against a dead wall. All his life lie has been
accustomed to reach out for the pies on other
people’s plates. He grabbed the Attorney-
Generalship ; he grabbed a Judgeship; Dibbs
aborted his reach-out for the Chief Justiceship;
but Judge Ventosior at last clutched the long-
coveted Sydney University Chancellorship. Then
there remained but one prize—the Chief Justice-
ship, carrying with it the Lieutenant-Governorship.
But Chief Justice Darley’s life looks a good one-
just as good as Judge Windeyer’s, for the chief is
a strong man, of a strong race, who isn’t a glutton,
looks not on the wine when it is red, and keeps
his liver in order by long marches in the moun-
tains. It was useless to wait for Judge Darley’s
shoes ; besides, even if the Chief Justiceship were
vacant, Barton would have a better chance than
Windeyer with the average Ministry. And so
Judge Windeyer bethought himself of a Bis-
marckian scheme—he would leap over Darley’s
head as it were by.being appointed to the Privy
Council. It hasn’t yet come off.

Habitue : “I have not seen noticed a
significant addition to the glass-case of relics,
at Melb. Public Library. This is a large parch-
ment donated by J. J. Walsh,_ who married
Johnny Eawkner’s widow. It is a. protest by
the Vic. Convention, headed by William Gray
and C. J. Don, against the Land Bill of 185?,
an outrageous thing which the Government

subsided into a comfortable merchant,” ,
The just-deceased S.A. Commissionef
of Police, William John Peterswald, known
colloquially as Herr von Peterswald, was
about as pure-bred a mongrel—speaking etv
mologically and not disrespectfully—as you
could find anywhere. He was of German
strain, but his father was a British army
officer. Peterswald was born in Jamaica,
was schooled in Jersey (English Channel),
and married a French lady. So that, all
told, he was a Germano-Franco-Anglo-Wesfc-
indian compound. He was also a good fellow
—for a policeman; a Mason and bon-vivani,
with an odd splash of the gambler thrown in ;
one who knew how to find his “bowels of
compassion”; a strict martinet; an ex-cap-
tain of militia —he was a captain soon aftpr
he was a (legal) major; influence made hha
an inspector of police, and supposed merit
passed him up to the Commissionership. A
mighty big wig in Adelaide was he, but in-
variably spoken of with that jocular familiaritv
that passes as a sign of popularity. Age.
nearly 67 years ; Australian experience, nearly
44; commissionerdom, over 14 years, but he
was the real head boss of police before that.
“ Peter ” was one of the numerous clan of
educated hard-ups from England who joined
S.A. police force in the early days.
Among the most fervid promoters of the
new Sydney corps of “Australian Rifles” is Mr.
R. A. Price, M.L.A. He will be an officer, no
doubt. But let him take notice that Australia
can only stand one Colonel Price,
Surely the demise of Charles Dickens, jun..
was better worth cabling than the doings in Lord
Nozoo’s back kitchen. Not a word was known
about it here till the mail came in ; but Australia
was duly told by wire that Lord Borndrunk of
England had condescended to promise to marry
the gilded Australian girl.

Judge Windeyer surely could not expecv
Premier Reid to extend his leave, and thus, by
provoking and giving opportunities for a bitte;
Parliamentary debate, bring down upon his (the
Premier’s) own head all the burning indignation
and sense of wrong which for years and years haw
been pent up in the public breast at the judicial
doings of Judge Mount Rennie. The grossness or
Mr. Windeyer’s bearing to G. H, Reid, the strug-
gling counsel who defended Abigail, will never fc
forgotten by anyone who witnessed it; and let it
here be said to the present Premier’s credit that hen
the only barrister, save E. P. Field, who ever pro-
perly faced-up W. C, Windeyer aforesaid in Ms
own court, and met the overbearing Judge’s boorist
rudeness with a sarcasm that made Baron Mouns
Rennie snort with rage. All things come to him
who waits. Lots of people besides the Right Hon
Baron Mount Rennie, P.C., picked up G. B.
Reid for a pug and found him to be a bull-pup.

“ I was afraid to send certain distinguishee

Pizzala v. Pizzala, London Divorce Court,
has just educed a momentous judgment. Th»
wife refused to live with her husband unless he
put away a mistress. She left him, and the Court
held that he had deserted her.
COHEN.
“ Mr. H. E. Cohen has been appointed a Judge of
N.S.W. Supreme Court in succession to Mr. Justice
Windeyer.”
It is Cohen, as you know,
Owns the three suspended balls ;
It is Cohen booms a show,
Boxes, circle and the stalls;
Ask the cabbies on the ranks,
It is Cohen who conducts ;
It is Cohen runs the banks,
It is Cohen reconstructs ;
It is Cohen takes the medal
At sassiety’s best flop ;
It is Cohen files his “ sheddle,”
Pays a “fink ” and comes out top ;
Twangs a “Ten-to-one, bar one ”?
It is through a Cohen nose ;
It is “ Cohen, cohen, co’n ! ”
When your furniture all goes ;
Cohen rules the “ ’Change ” and stocks;
Cohen scents the “ wild-cat” stench ;
Cohen’s in the witness-box;
Cohen’s on the Judgment Bench !
To his death and from his birth,
Cohen rules the blessed earth.

Dear Bulletin,—Only a very
few Queenslanders are responsible for
having Premier Hugh Nelson in Parlia-
ment at all. He was returned mostly
by the station - vote where men are
tviw vote against the boss. The
ballot does not protect them; or if it
does they do not think so-for good
reasons, probably. Out of 14 polling
places, nearly all are stations. Two
places, Goondmindi and Toowoomba,
ar ® t^le electorate altogether;
yet both were polling-places where ID
property-votes were registered for Nel-
onlv aI T t n ? lle represents
only 298 votes. In fact, Nelson has
fewer votes than any other man in the
Q. legislature.— H.S.R.

Dear Bulletin, -Re the fact that saffron-
yellow was the old Hibernian national color, it’s
not generally known that the harp has only been
the Irish emblem since Henry VIII., after his
row with the Pope, substituted it on the Royal
Standard for the original “Three Crowns,” lest
these might be mistaken for the Triple Tiara of
the Papacy.—A. M.

The very latest prescription for growing a
splendid mass of hair is not to wash the head for
two years. Apparently the great Gospel of Dirt is
coming into vogue again.
A late Q. Parliamentary return shows that
the leases of Barcaldine, Bimerah, Clar'e, Peak
Downs, and Wolfgang cover 983,080 acres, and
that the rent in 1890 came to =£1721165. Id., rising
until 1895, when it reached =£2ool Bs. lOd. That
is, the Government received a ha’penny an acre
for this vast territory in the last year and less for
the years preceding. When the expense of collect-
ing and other incidentals are deducted, Queens-
land would appear to be practically charging no
rent for an immense area of rolling-downs country.
The true story of a man from Somerset
(Eng.) with an English father and a Welsh mother.
He was in London and wanted to get out of it,
and, having no funds, he stowed away on a Maori-
land meat steamer. When he landed in Lyttleton
the captain gave him half-a-crown and said: I lie
Lord be good to you.”* The man from Zorner-
zet ” looked at the awful town of Lyttleton, at
the half-crown, and at a TJ.S.S. Co.’s boat with
the blue peter flying (and he was from Zomerzet,
mind you, and no Jew), and then walked up to the
biscuit-factory on the hill and asked : “ Yhere be
the maaster ? ” “ That’s me ! ” said a brisk-look-
ing man. “ Gimme two shillins’ worth o bis-
cuits.” “Oh, go to a shop—we only sell em
wholesale here." “ Thaat’s all right, Ibe goin’ to
zell ’em agen.” ‘‘ Oh, all right,” said the biscuit-
man—“give him 121 b. weight.” Then theZomer-
zet man hied him to a lolly-shop and bought six-
penn’orth of little paper-bags, and into each
paper-bag he put six biscuits. Then to the U. S. S.
Co.’s steamer, where he sold the lot for £5, at a
shilling a bag, as sure sea-sick-preventive biscuits.
And he was from England, look you, from Zomer-
zet; and now he is a capitalist such as the King-
dom of Maoriland is made of.
Hermes : “I saw an old woman staggering
up William-street, Sydney; the other day, when a
small boy kicked a little paper parcel into the
crossing and out flew a sandwich. A dog snapped
the meat, the boy trod one slice of bread under
foot, and the old woman, dropping her worn-out
umbrella, snatched up the other slice, hid it under
her shawl, and glanced round fearfully lest any-
one had seen. Then she picked up her umbrella
and staggered on.”
Dear Bulletin,—No man with any sense
of the congruities should by choice travel over the
Canadian Pacific Railway. The Yankees are ac-
cused of defacing the scenery of their country
with advts. of pills and patent-medicines, but this
is not so bad as perpetuating the names of the
venal projectors of a semi-Govt. railroad by
naming the grandest scenery on its route after
them. Two of the finest peaks in the Canadian
Rockies are dubbed respectively Mount Stephen
and Mount Macdonald. Has anyone m Australia
ever heard of Lord Mount Stephen ? He is the
first blossom of a Colonial peerage and the
origin of his absurd title is worth telling. Origi-
nally a Montreal softgoods-man, he staked his pile
on the Canadian Pacific project, spent part ot it
where it did the most good, generally about elec-
tion times and in the interests of Macdonald s
Government; got the road through, and named
two of the Western Continent's most majestic
scenes after Macdonald and himself. 1 or. his
“ disinterested and patriotic services ” he received
a peerage, whatever that may be, and is now-
think of the wonderful apotheosis of the creature
and the hideous desecration of nature!—Lord
Mount Stephen. — Kanuck.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
fhethree slogging amateurs—Palairet, Jackson,
and O Brien—won the Scarborough match for Thornton's
h even making 203 among them out of a 292 total.
1 iid tb ® team, which was about the strongest
All-England combination for wet weather, achieved 80 runs
tor / wickets, a slightly worse average than the Aus-
trahans. Peel, who was “ nowhere ” among the bowlers
in the early months of the season, canie out on this occa-
sion with a record of 12 for 96, whilst J. T. Hearne took
only 4 wickets for 114. George Smith took 3 for 19. In
one or two other matches Smith did pretty well against
the visitors, so he must have, in a small way, the best
boss bowling average for Anglo-Australian matches.
* * * *
“Trott,” writes an Australian abroad to The
Bulletin, “ has proved himself an admirable captain, both
in the field and out of it, and, without loss of command or
dignity, has managed to keep the members of the team in
good temper all round. Giffen declares him to be the
best skipper he has ever played under, and even the
English press admit that he quite out-generaled W.G. in
the second test-match.
“ It is surprising, when a big victory is on the
cards, to observe how the Australian colors are to the
fore. On the third day of the second test-match, and
again at Surrey, all sorts of unknown youths were swag-
gering about with blue and gold hatbands and the coat-
ot-arms embroidered on them, also with blue and gold ties
of all sorts. If, by accident, a stranger takes them for
members of the team, they are delighted, and never un-
deceive anyone.
Lohman sent a wire at the last moment to say
he was not well enough to play against the Australians in
the first Surrey match. Rumor has it that he thought
his appearance would tend to spoil the interest
taken in his benefit three days later, and that he did
not wish to ‘show’ in public before that event. If
this was so it was a poor compliment to the Australians,
who had given £2O towards this benefit, and his non-
appearance certainly affected the gate, though it was a
big one even without him. It was noticeable, too, that
though too ill to play he was well enough to be present in
the pavilion, working energetically for the success of his
own match.
“On each occasion when the Australians have
played at Lord’s they have started apparently in a
blue funk. They certainly have always recovered as the
game went on, but, on the first two occasions, too late to
draw o/it ° n only just in time to make a
‘‘One of the finest photos, ever taken is that of
the Sydney Cncket Ground, which hangs in the bar of
Lord s Pavilion. It is about Bft. long and 2ft. high and
was taken during the great test-match against Stoddart’s
team when the Australians just lost by seven runs.
“ Clem. Hill, who has bo frequently got out
during the tour in endeavouring to pull straight and even
balls on the off stump round to leg, made nearly all his
118 runs against Surrey in that way. He had announced
his intention of keeping up the stroke which had so often
but A t ls P art of his style and he can’t
resist the temptation. He seems to be sure enough of it
when well set, but not before, although at Birmingham

* * * *
An automatic contrivance for inflating pneu-
matm tyres in general has recently been invented and
perfected by Mr. W. Merton, of Marrickville, Sydney
Its simplicity and effectiveness will popularise it through-
°ubthe world. The invention is so fixed on a cycle-wheel
that while the wheel protects it from injury it sustains
the wheel s balance, and as its weight is a mere trifle it
dispenses with the consideration of decreased speed • it is
impervious to water and dirt, is noiseless and practically
everlasting and without causing the cyclist to dismount
it can be set at work or at rest by the touch of a finger.
Up to now, if a cyclist wanted to blow his tyre up he got
off and walked back ten miles to where he had lost his
pump, or thought he had lost it, or he borrowed another
pump which wouldn’t fit, with the result that instead of
riding the cycle, the cycle rode him to the nearest drinkerv
where dinner was over. But the automatic pump of Mr
Merton will change all this. Perhaps its best feature is
that if a tyre is punctured by half-a-dozen tacks the
pump constantly working will maintain so strong an in-
jection of air that the service of the tyre is preserved •
while the pump’s wonderful efficiency should revolution-
ise the slow adaptation of the pneumatic tyre for buggies
c *’ P4 m Pi n £ trouble now being reduced to
0. The inventor will patent the invention in England
and America and will then place it upon the market at a
small cost.

* * « *
That relay race between Sydney and Melbourne
is only one of many schemes got up to advertise the un-
interesting Ego of a certain very pushing young man.
Sfcanbury, under 30 years of age, and in “splen-
did condition,” downed by Gaudaur, aged 39. It is Beach
v. Gaudaur over again ; the elder sculler beating the
younger. But what" stuff Gaudaur must be made of, and
what a champion of champion’s Bill Beach was to down
such an opponent ! A sculling race is one of the severest
ordeals into which a man can enter, and these victories of
40 year old youths over green boys of 30 adds fresh sensa-
tion to the uncertain, ever-changing history of athletics.
Some matured man of 50 should now arise and give
Gaudaur his quietus.
* * * •
The Sydney syndicate which cabled a few hun-
dred for investment on big Jim hasn’t shown out since.
And it is pretty safe to say that some of the Aus. Eleven,
wishing to double their earnings, turned out in style and
went home stone-broke. Stanbury was probably troubled
with insomnia the night previous, and fell asleep during
the race.
A 20-lengths win practically means beaten out
of sight. How the Hinglishers will glory in Stanbury’s
discomfiture ! Gaudaur has apparently been conserving
his strength for years ; training quietly, and living a
model life. He is more brilliant than the Ryde giant, who
has seemingly been ruined by over-confidence.
World’s-champion Gaudaur used to beat and be
beaten by Teenier every month. He is 39, and Stanbury
29. Beach was 46 last Sunday.

The fact that Nada, knocked over by a bike on
Randwick-road, will be able to start at the A. J.C. gave
much satisfaction to the St. Alban’s stable. Stable was
also glad to hear that the bike was smashed to atoms.
Neither jockey Morrison nor the scorching middle-of-the-
roader was injured. But the language used would have
broken an iron-bar.

Nada is the first racehorse in the world to get
knocked down by a bicyclist. “ Coincidence ’’-seekers
will possibly regard this as a tip for next Melbourne Cup,
because the fact that the mare smashed the bike may
mean that she is going to break the record. What price
Nada, anyhow?

The Taranaki representative 15 is again the
champion M.L. football-team, having won all the inter-
provincial matches this year—the third year in succession
it has gone through the season without a defeat. This
year (says a Bulletin correspondent) will probably see
the retirement of Alf. Bayly, Allan and Hugh Good, and
a Taranaki team minus these will be hardly known. No
less than seven Bayly brothers have played for the pro-
vince, and Alf. has played in its matches continuously
since 85. Four of the Good family have represented
Taranaki, Allan first playing in ’B7. This year’s team,
which is almost as strong as that of ’94, contains no less
than 13 farmers, some of them living nearly 100 miles
ftpart.

i-n'k® sport-love of Australian youth is going to
still further express itself in “Australian Cricket—a
weekly, record of the game.” T. W. Garrett, C. T. B.
Turner, A. C. K. Mackenzie, umpire “Teddy” Briscoe
and one or two smaller cricket authorities will contribute
to the new venture, and should make it popular. That
lS’if they can write cricket as well as they can play it.
Judged by the result of the test-matches, Australia will
have to play more and talk less cricket than formerly
and it is to be hoped that the new paper will show this
country s youths how to do so.

plumps stra 'ght for little Dalmeny. And a record will
have to be broken to keep him out of a place.

win in this event.

* • * •
It doesn’t always follow that the acceptance of
a sweep-cut by an owner means that he is going to try.
In one case awhile back the nag wasn’t touched in town
or on the course by the stable, and the public kept off
with wonderful unanimity. And he belongs to an
allegedly “straight” establishment. As a set-off, the
writer must mention that the same owner on another
occasion wasn’t “ approached ” by the ticketholder, and
said he didn’t care. He was going to start and win. And
he did.

Only a jock, of Lewis’ calibre could have pulled
off the forlorn hope on Thunder Queen in S.A. J.C. Derby,
a brisk, turbulent race in which a dead set was made on
the favorite. Concussion left, Eleusinian crossed, Helena
kicked, Thunder Queen blocked and then bumped by
The Royalist at a critical moment is an ugly catalogue for
a classic field of seven. Although Lewis won amid a babel
of sound, the concluding incident should teach him a
useful lesson re the folly of trying to sneak up on the
inside. r

Chris. Moore (he got his license renewed the
other week, after nearly two years of standing down) has
been always credited with more skill than seemed good
for his reputation. People used to talk of him as a
magician, and the result of this was a public snorting and
shaking of the head if the overrated jockey rode a bad
race. To the present writer’s way of thinking, Chris.
Moore_ has ridden some bad races with perfectly good
intentions, and, a 3 for the incident which brought him to
grief, he wasn’t to blame for it.
* * * *
Por a real, gilt-edged, aristocratic and, at the
same time, exciting, international contest, a series of
Australia v. India polo matches would be exactly the
thing. The only stumbling-block is the fact that the
games differ; the Anglo-Indians playing the English
game somewhat after the manner of Rugby football,
while Australian polo follows the method of scoring and
playing associated with the native football. Another diffi-
culty would be the choosing of a representative team so
as to prevent the Cuff-and-Collar crowd monopolising the
places to the exclusion of the Geebungs,

* * * •
W. Davison is one of the most muscular athletes
yevseen upon a wheel; he is 23 years old, sft. 6in. high,
list. 71b. in condition. He has a magnificently developed
trunk and a pair of huge thighs. Davison’s fine physique
is the result of his work as a miner and farmer. He
learnt to ride only 12 months ago, and after three weeks
learning won a mile and a half race with 180yds. start,
and came second in a ten-mile race; he would have been
first but for falling. Before coming to the League’s
Autumn meeting to astonish even Bill Martin himself,
Davison, starting from scratch each time, won the whole
programme at Emmaville, N.S.W. ; viz., the half-mile,
mile, mile and a half, two miles, and three miles. Like
Bill. Martin, he does not drink or smoke. Davison is a
native of Hillgrove, N.S.W.
* * * *
A bike expert to The Bulletin --—Among the
horrors of the street are the bike-women who perch them-
selves on their wheels so as to make an obtuse angle,
with their insteps on the pedals, kicking straight out in
front, scaring dogs, tramcars, men, and horses. Others
have their saddles too low, and seem intensely interested
ln the dirt on the front wheel. To ride gracefully, the
saddle should be placed so that the rider can hold the
handles comfortably while sitting bolt upright, and the
ball of the foot should just rest upon the pedal when the
leg is at full stretch. Most women work their pedals like
prisoners on a treadmill—straight up and down —whereas
a graceful ankle-action is the essential sign of an accom-
plished cycliste. When the leg is fully extended and the
foot begins to rise the pedal should be clawed up with it
—brought up with a sweep, as it were. Done naturally,
it is wonderful how this increases the ease, grace, and
speed. To make pace, the handles should be gently
pressed towards the chin, as though trying to lift them,
and the pedals worked faster and not harder.

■kit ! he English jockey, Brown, now riding at

that Tom Cannon and other old masters teach their

Champion Memmott and Joe Byrne gave a long

Melbourne, last week. Memmott, who conceded only
1000 in 5000, seemed to be playing to make an interesting
game of it, but he didn’t succeed.

CANNALIN CURE FOR PILES.
It may not be generally known that this remark-
able remedy is the preparation of a Sydney botanic
druggist, Mr. Albert Sharp, who, for some nine
years, has carried on a consulting business at
257 George-street, Sydney. Among many well-
known people it has cured may be mentioned Mr.
Thos. Spratt, of Hargraves, who was a dreadful
sufferer for 17 years; Mr. Mulholland, of Elong
Elong (18 years); and Mr. A. Rendell, of Top
Bingara (15 years). The charge of One Pound for
the six weeks’ course of treatment, carriage paid
to any colony, is a very reasonable one, largely
appreciated by many who could ill-afford more.
Copies of the 3rd edition of “ Piles and How to
Cure Them,” by Albert Sharp, can be obtained
free on application.*

* • * *
The paucity of jockeys who can ride at all
decently is being commented upon in the old strain up
country. As soon as a promising reinsman learns the way
he and his steed should go he invades the dazzling metro-
polis to start a brilliant career riding stiffs at pony
scrambles. There are hundreds of assorted steering pig-
mies in tight-fitting togs and dashaway hats around
Sydney whose method of living would furnish a startling
article. The majority are often glad enough to dine
sumptuously at a fourpenny chop-and-sausage factory.
And when they can’t raise 4d. they sell their waiskets, or
go without.
* * * *
There is a revival in Melb. matters aquatic.
Young Gid. Palmer, the St. Kilda enthusiast, is having a
5-rater built by the well-known Auckland builders, C. and
W. Bailey, with which he hopes to lower Gov. Brassey’s
colors in Nov. The new boat, a beautiful model, is 46ft.
long overall, 30ft. along waterline, with a beam of 9ft. 6in.,
and is to sail under the name of Uira, which in the Maori
tongue signifies lightning.
* * * *
On July 18th, at New York, a 15-hands horse
called Tycho Brahe jumped 7ft. 6Jin., breaking the world’s
record.
* * * •
Miles, the V.R.O. identity, is taking a six
months’ leave of absenoe, and his probable retirement is,
for no apparent reason, hinted at. The square ‘‘old”
man is well on the right side of sixty, a mere juvenile as
identities go, and it would be a pity for him to leave a gap
behind the V.R.O. office-counter for a thinner man to fill.
The strong contrast between secretary Byron Moore and
chief-clerk Miles strikes one as a desirable thing in busi-
ness management. The sec., utterly destitute of stummick
and taste for racing, is long-necked and suave. Miles is
the serious embodiment of sport, with lots of stummick, a
very short neck, and a somewhat short temper, which
serves to keep the office freejrom hangers-round. Yet an
obliging informant, with all.

Doctors begin to ukase that girls must not
clean their teeth.
Melb. Argus* in a leader on Macdonald,
the nudity man, mentions that Mac. claims to
have the sympathy of, amongst others, “Mr.
Edward Carpenter, whoever he may be.” To be
ignorant of the literature of the modern move-
ments it criticises is the misfortune of the Argus,
but to proclaim that ignorance thus is silly. Car-
penter’s poem, “Towards Democracy,” and his
book “ England’s Ideal ” contain the very essence
of the modern social movement.
“Diamond cut diamond.” Queensland
publican gives a patron one of his own cheques in
exchange for another cheque. Publican discovers
this cheque to be valueless; hooks it off to the
bank to stop his own cheque from being cashed.
Too late. It has been cashed, but —the man had j
been given two of Mine Host’s dishonored cheques
and only 2s. Gd. change! Hard luck that the pub.
patron should afterwards be prosecuted for “false
pretences ”!
The curse of Govt. Commissions. The
members of the Local Govt. Commission in Bris-
bane, who were receiving dfil Is. a day (and 10s.
hotel-allowance for country members), struck a
few weeks ago for £2 2s. daily. The strike lasted
a day, when Govt, caved in. Total cost of Com-
mission (just an excuse for junketing Ministerial
supporters in the metropolis—a 'sort of minor
C.M.G.-ship), .£2OOO. (By the way, some of the
strikers strongly advocate the hanging of all labor
agitators who stir up the workers into rebellion.)
