Maoriland Parliament has recently had
under discussion a measure for the repression of
juvenile immorality. Whereupon the parsons have
raised the warwhoop from the pulpit, and rung the
tocsin from the steeple. “See to what your God-
less system of education has brought you ! ” But
it won’t do. Do they seriously believe that the
evil could be removed by half-an-hour’s perfunc-
tory catechism per diem ? Or are the passions of
the young “colonial ” to be subdued and mortified
by a close study of Hosea ? Or are the chosen
sheep to be yarded off (at Govt, expense) from the
goats outside ? It is perhaps well to remember
that in Europe the purlieus of a cathedral are
generally the local Alsatia.
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclougli,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot of course be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough.*
Circular received by Sydney doctor :
Mrs. , having lately moved to , on the Southern
line, would be glad to accommodate a lady or gentleman
needing a comfortable country home. The air in is
very invigorating; and as Mrs. has a buggy and a
cow, boarders could be driven to and from the station and
.about the country, and they would be able to have plenty
of fresh milk and cream. Delicate persons would receive
every care and attention.
Belgium is trying to do away with Sunday
postal delivery. All new stamps have a coupon
attached, on which the words “not to be delivered
on Sundays ” are printed. If a person desires to
have letters delivered on Sunday or any other day,
the coupon must be torn off.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
Tasmania has celebrated its Arbor-day.
Enthusiasm at the function was not violently ex-
pressed, perhaps because, in a well-timbered
country, familiarity with gum-trees and “ horizon-
tal ” scrub breeds contempt for ornamental tree-
planting. A powerful stump-extractor at work
rooting up the surrounding forest would appeal to
the sensibilities of Tasmanian school children
infinitely more than the ordinary Arbor-day flum-
mery and the speeches of local aldermen. Forest
scenery is already photographed on the very souls
of the rising generation of Tasmanians. The
young people of both sexes find cheap and harm-
less Sunday and holiday amusement in clearing
patches of scrub and building well-appointed
mia-mias among the ferns and wattles of seques-
tered gullies adjacent to the towns. Round Hobart
and Launceston there are dozens of “fairy
bowers,” “ grass-tree huts,” and other more or less
appropriately-named sylvan retreats —standing
monuments to the artistic bashfulness of Tas-
manian lads and lasses, who appear to know just
as much about arbors as is good for any respect-
able community.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The vagaries of Party Government. When
the recent plebiscite motion came on in N.S.W.
Assembly, the Reid Ministry decided with much
firmness to denounce it and vote against it.
Whereupon the Opposition unexpectedly resolved
to support it —and a Govt, defeat was imminent.
But the secret was badly kept, and when the
Opposition’s intention leaked out, the Ministry
promptly faced about and decided to support it,
while the Opposition somersaulted and determined
to vote against it. Premier Reid, the_ cynics say,
was just warming up in his denunciation when
Syd. Smith (the managing Tom Slattery of the
Freetraders) rushed breathlessly in and gave his
chief the “ tip.” Whereupon Reid backed water
and steered right round this awkward rock. The
solidarity labor-men usually answer the crack of
the Freetrade whip, but for once in their existence
they cracked the whip for both parties. Also,
leading articles for the daily papers, written and
set up upon the assumption that the Govt, would
oppose the plebiscite, were blue-pencilled, and the
ready writers who had written the proposal down
set about writing it up with all possible despatch.
Eno’s “ Fruit Salt.”— ln pimples and blotches
on the face, sallowness of the skin, and depression
of spirits, it is most useful, for not the least of its
recommendations is its resemblance to fruit in the
natural way in which it relieves the system of
effete or poisonous matter, which, if retained,
poisons the blood ; and its advantages over fruit
are that it can be always at hand when required.
Its preparation has been truly styled one of the
triumphs of modern chemistry. In hot or foreign
climates it is invaluable. It allays nervous excite-
ment, and restores the nervous system to its proper
condition (by natural means). In the nursery it
is beyond praise. Sold by all chemists and stores.*
Writes “Another Shark” :
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos “Divorce by Mutual Con-
sent,” please print this rather pedantic note. Divorce is
not, and never has been, “in the eyes of the law merely
a civil contract.” In considering the subject of
“ marriage,” a careful distinction must be drawn between
(1) An agreement to marry, which is a contract, (2)
Marriage, the fact or ceremony, civil or religious, which
induces the status of (3) Matrimony or marriage. This
last—matrimony—is not a contract but a sfafws, and one
of the distinguishing marks of a status is that you cannot
vary by contract the special rights and duties attaching
to it. Marriage can be ended only by judicial process;
but the most important characteristic distinguishing it
from a contract is, that the incidents attaching to it are
fixed by a uniform rule of law and depend in no sense
whatever upon the agreement of the parties. These two
attributes of the marriage relation take it completely out
of the category of any contracts known to English law.
I have no particular objection to divorce being granted
by mutual consent; but there is no need to base the
argument for it on a false analogy.

Writes “ Comp.” The advantage of
machinery is cheapness of production and
consequent low price of resultant, thereby
bringing the article within the purchasing
power of a larger circle. This does not
apply to the linotype. Tor instance, the daily
papers will not be reduced in price, nor will the
cost of advertising; but the proprietors’ profits
will be enormously increased at the expense of the
discharged compositor. In fact, this invention
sucks the public and spoon-feeds Fat Man.

The Sydney Lager Beer Brewing Co.,
floated by Messrs. Jones and Black, threatens to
be a huge success. The beer brewed by the system
in Melbourne (and now being sold by Mr. James
Kidman in Sydney) is really undistinguishabie
from first-class German beer, and will be retailed,
when the Sydney brewery begins operations at
about half the price of imported. Also, it will be
supplied only to hotels which contract to draw it
through the company’s ice-machines. Sydney
pants for cheap, light beer in hot weather ; to
drink the ordinary heavy beer is simply to poison
yourself. The Australian bent is all lor light
liquor, and The Bulletin predicts that within
five years the other breweries will be compelled to
add lager-plants to their establishments.

HT-Pf 3,1 * -? UELETIN > —-Re Centralian frogs.
While travelling far out some years ago with a
black boy our water became exhausted, and we
jT-di 6 01n ® a perish ” when the boy informed me
Plenty water sit down.” Proceeding to a mat
oi mulga leaves that had collected in a depression,
he turned them oyer and disclosed a number of
bulky frogs. Taking a twig in one hand and a
pannikin in the other, he began a rapid prodding
oi the frogs, causing them to jump and discharge
their cargo of water, which he dexterously caught
in the pannikin. In a minute or two he presented
me with a drink of clear, cold water. I drank it
all right.— Percy Hodskinson, L.S. [Next.—

A Leader of Labor .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
108 Lavender Hill, Battersea, London, is a section
of a two-storeyed terrace as modest-looking as pro-
truding bow-windows will allow. In a dark
corner of the porch a neat, one-inch-by-five brass
slip bears the name “John Burns.”
Short to undersizedness, of a tough, hairy type,
with a clean-trimmed beard—not black tinged
with grey, but grey tinged with black —there is
little in this man’s appearance to tell the passer-by
he is the foremost fighting leader in the van of the
workers’ progress. The “ windows of the soul ”
have the blinds down, from behind which the man
waits in his strong impressiveness the time to
speak, and meanwhile holds his tongue. Clear, as
the sky after a thunderstorm, the eyes disclose
nothing, though they hint at hidden force. _ The
beard covers the cheek-bones, and a stray wisp or
two have escaped and acutely curved themselves
like boomerangs, thick in the centre, over the eyes.
Looks seafaring, and might be taken for the cap-
tain of a coaster, A low, open collar conceals a
scarf with Leighton-tied endSj and discloses a V
of hairy chest. Dark jacket suit, quiet gold chain,
and rough, heavy shoes complete an unassuming
appearance. llis. manner is genial, but not
effusive, and calculated to put either a Park Laner
or a Seven Dialer at' home and at ease.
He says: “ The World is getting better because
the condition of the poor is improving. What is
the first thing that strikes a visitor in the London
streets ? ” The first thing that struck me was one
of the many low-hung awnings, and the next the
absence of the poverty-parched wretches I had
expected to see. P’r’aps they were kept in the
back streets where the awnings should be ! “No;
the lot of the people has improved a great deal
within the last ten years.” It might improve 200
per cent, and yet be a very little “lot” m Eng-
land. _ , ,
“ The Chartered Company must go and .Rhodes
with it. He is like Parnell and a few others who
want to be not only leaders but bosses, and
Britishers of to-day won’t stand that. The day
of bosses is past.”
Consider the English form of government the
finest and comparatively freest on earth. Nothing
but a very improved form of republic. (By-the-
way, his voice rings through that cramped ornate
biscuit-box —the House of Commons like the
clear, steady sound of a tolling bell. It demands
and obtains respectful silence.)
The conduct of Labor matters in Australia has
not impressed him favorably. “American Labor
leaders are bad enough, but Antipodean worse,
Many Labor leaders come from a type of self-
styled journalists—not industrious enough to be
penny-a-liners and not clever enough to be any-
thing better. Here, they delight to worm them-
selves into Labor Circles, and get a following by
recounting petty details of my life, or pointing out
my mistakes, and how they would remedy them.
He asked about Trenwith, Maloney, Hancock,
Murphy, and Fitzgerald, and received varying
values of the capabilities and possibilities of each
individual. , , . ,
“ The ‘ tip ’ system in England is decreasing as
rapidly as it is increasing in America. It has
always sapped the self-respect and manliness of
the workers, and my opposition to the penny-
pride-purchase lost me 1800 votes at the last
general, and will lose me the ‘police’ and post-
men’ at the next, for I’m going to crush the Xmas-
box business.” -
“Lord? Well, I find the average ‘lord. a
gentlemanly, well-informed, simple-speaking,
simple-living man. We have over a score on the
London City Council—as fine fellows as anyone
would wish to meet.” Good “ lord ” !
MINYARAH.
At the race-club ball at Hannan’s the men
outnumbered the women by 12 to 1, and the “ real
lydies” made it a strict rule not to dance with
any man who had previously on the same evening
skipped round with a barmaid.
' One Body, a N.S.W. attorney, has been
disciplined for failing to account (ahem !) to clients.
Pass the lyre
Gin a body meet a Body
In his little lair —
Gin a Body rob a body,
Need a body swear V
Ilka body has a lawyer, ,
Nane hae I, I wist;
But if I had, and he robbed me,
I’d hae him off the list.
A lady professor of walking advertises in
Melb.
'Melb.' : “The women have been crowding
to-day round a straw hat, in a Bourke-street
drapery window. It could be placed on the head,
and yet bore first-class imitation parsnips, toma-
toes, onions, walnuts, a little cabbage, pome-
granate, carrots, asparagus, ears of corn, a bit of
cauliflower, &c., surmounted by rosebuds.” It
would have looked well on Eve in Eden where she
wore naught else.
Canon Potter, of Melb., is quite certain
lying is not necessary in business, and is sure there
are “many men in business who would rather
starve<than lie.” In these days of adulteration a
business-man must lie, and the man who wants to
tell the truth as a steady thing is as much out of
place behind the counter as he would appear to be
in the pulpit where the parsons preach the Trewth
that shames the very devil.
The Boodlewraith’s Chinese statistics are
amusing. According to them only one. Chow
crossed into Queensland in 1890; only three in 1893;
and in 1896 only 38. The Boodlewraith admits
that out of 145 who crossed ,in six years, only
three paid the fine of =£so apiece. The Banana-
land statistics are compiled on the simple, happy
assumption that every Chow immigrating into
Queensland immediately makes for the nearest
police-station, hands himself over to the authori-
ties, and begs to be mulcted in the sum of <£so, or
packed back straightway over the border.
Dear Bulletin,— The other day I an-
swered a lawyer’s advt. “Wanted a clerk,” and
counted 115 applicants of all sorts. Now, I hear
that this “gent one” has the reputation of only
“engaging” a clerk on “ appro, for a month, with-
out salary. Salary (often unstated) to commence
if appro’d ! ” It is further alleged, too, that, for a
year past, the gentleman has been unable to
“appro.” any applicant he has tried; has been
unable, therefore, to pay a clerk a salary ; and has
been obliged to put up with the gratis services of
clerks, each from three to four weeks at a time.—
Once Bit.
Another Australian abroad :
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos the British tub, continents
ablutionary arrangements are even worse than those in
England. The best hotels in Italy and Switzerland, always
monopolised by English tourists during summer, rarely
have more than one bath-room—generally on the first
bed-room floor. Those of the second and third-class
sport mostly none at all. This refers, of course, only to
town hotels. In the country a bath-room is an unknown
quantity. The Britisher who dotes onhistub at home seems
to “ stow ’’ this laudable habit when abroad. A supply of
sand-paper costs probably less than a bath at Is. When I
was a boy tennis, football, cricket, &c., were little, or not
at all, played on the continent. The ever-increasing in-
flux of English visitors during summer hag, however,
made these games popular in many parts. The “ tub ”is
left as of yore. English influence could prevail upon
continentals to take to British games, &• c., but at the tub
they still draw the line. Really, one has to go back to the
dear old mother country to appreciate the full worth of
the beastly colonies.
According to the Maoriland Bill for Paw-
ing the Young Female after 10 p.m., any girl
apparently under 16 found a second time in the
streets after 10 at night may be sent to a reforma-
tory. The first time she is merely liable to be in-
terrogated by the policeman on the beat with the
assistance of the “cop” on the next beat whom
he is to call in to his aid, and led to the house of
a clergyman, J.P., or householder of good repute,
and examined as to her name, parentage, address,
age, reasons for being out, &c., and then led home
by the wrist and delivered to her parents or
guardians, along with a statement of the circum-
stances. The second time, however, she may be
“ sent up ” for several years. Evidently there . is
going to be a bad time in Maoriland for the girl
apparently under 16.
Yic. Justice Williams, in suspending a
dishonest lawyer the other day, said : “ The time
is fast approaching when these gentlemen should
not be suspended, but struck off the rolls at onoe.
It is time that this misappropriation—purloining,
I might call it—by trustees generally of their
clients’ moneys should be put a stop to.” The
phenomenal unkindness of his Honor’s comment
lies in the suggestion that he might apply the term
“ purloining ”to the deed of embezzlement. This
comes of riding a bike, The Bulletin supposes.
The machine has shaken up learned brother
Williams’ liver to such a degree that he can’t keep
his mind from grasping the situation. Pretty soon
he will begin to fancy that a gentleman who de-
serves to be struok off the rolls is tantamount to a
mere person who ought to be in gaol like other
thieves.
To keep milk sweet, cool it immediately
after it comes from the cow, thus killing the bac-
teria of fermentation before they get to work.
Good-conduct prisoners are daily employed
driving the Pentridge (Melb.) gaol-carts in and
out of Melbourne, along the Sydney-road. The
men wear the hideous regulation dress, _ are duly
numbered and lettered on the backs of their jackets,
and seemingly enjoy the outing, evidently per-
fectly callous to the observation of tram passengers
and pedestrians. ‘ Retrenchment! ’
G. B. Shaw, speaking of Jerumky Jerum’s
“ Biarritz,” says : “ Witty things can no doubt
be said about prostitution, but prostitution in
itself is not a witty thing.” Apropos, some Vic-
torian vignerons were having a social on a_ recent
evening, and old Pierre had promised to
sing. He was called on but protested that he had
a sore throat. They would take no denial. At
last: “Well, I will get a substitute to sing for
me.” “What!” “Miss , she has kindly
consented to act the substitute for me.” Uproar.
Poor old Pierre didn’t. say substitute, but he
meant to.
The first volume of a cheap series of
Marcus Clarke’s sketchy works, published by his
widow and dedicated to the Australian public,
contains his much-quoted guess at the Future
Australian Race —a clever, inconsistent, and not
very complimentary prophesy. Some of the
author’s cause-and-effect deductions look sound
to-day, others are already proved false. “ The
present custom of drinking alcohol to excess will
continue,” he said. “All carnivora are rash,
gloomy, given to violence. Vegetarians live at a
lower level of health, but are calmer and happier.
Red radicals are for the most part meat-eaters.
. . . Fish-eaters are invariably moderate Whigs.
The Australians will be content with-nothing short
of a turbulent democracy.” But since Clarke’s
death a sober—well-nigh teetotal —race of natives
is growing up, and our democracy is the most
easy-going, police-fearing democracy on earth.
Melbourne, for instance, seems incapable of get-
ting excited under circumstances that would breed
riots in any big European city.
Dear Bulletin, — Be aboriginal supersti-
tions, prayers for rain, ethnological coincidences,
and various other things. Among the generation
of Victorian blackfellows which, in its younger
days, had witnessed the disappointing resurrection
of its forefathers, the most striking and most care-
fully-observed rite was the burning of feathers,
hair, or fur in time of drought. The lubras
attended to this, among other matters ; but I have
often heard my father say that when the feathers,
&c., failed to bring ram, the “coolies” (pre-
diggings term) used to set fire to their own hair
and beards. Whether this indisputably sincere
appeal had any effect on the clerk, no person who
has not mastered the relation of mind to matter
is fully qualified to judge. But those who claim
that our own “days of humiliation” or “weeks
of prayer ” are more efficacious, must have
queer ideas of the Being they think they
worship. Such, however, was the most con-
spicuous observance of the Port Phillip blacks.
It is not noticed by any writer I have happened to
meet with. Even Brough Smyth, in his elaborate
work on the aborigines of Victoria, seems to have
missed the information altogether. But, writing
from a childish, though perfectly distinct recollec-
tion, I have times out of number seen each old
lubra of a party, immediately on arriving at our
hut, carefully deposit on the fire her handful ol
feathers, wool, etc., collected on the way. I ins,
of course, would be in dry weather; in wet
weather, they were just as solicitous to see that no
rain-bringing material was burned. And, as the
poor creatures were seldom satisfied with the
weather-legislation, their harmless oblation was
too ordinary to excite comment. Is this peculiar
custom entirely Overlooked or forgotten by all
except one, who, like Sir John (not Madden), is
old only in judgment and understanding ? Meston
ought to know something about it. — -Tom Collins.

The blunders, amusing and the reverse,
caused through the general non-acquaintance of
the N.S.W. Civil servants with the members of
the new Public Service Board, suggest reminis-
cences of the advent to power of Templeton,
Irving and Couchman, as Public Service commis-
sioners in Victoria. When the triumvirate were
hard at work investigating and re-grading, say, in
the North East, they would be largely advertised
in the Melbourne press as being out West, and
when they were actually engaged out West, it
would be currently reported that one or two of
them were occupied with suburban classifications,
and the rest of the board was away on the Murray
border. The object of this, of course, was to put
peccant public officers off their guard, and as re-
sult surprises and discomfitures were not at all
infrequent until the dodge was seen through, and
various public officers within a certain district
devised a means of defence against the common
enemy. The three commissioners in a body
struck an important Western sea-board town one
day, and on their arrival, straightway entered the
Post and Telegraph Office. Couchman, an ex-
perienced electrician, led the way, and was just
in time to distinguish the transmission on the
sounder of a message which was to put every other
Western town on the circuit on the alert.” Just
got word from T that they’re on their way
here. Look out.” This was the message as sent,
and instantly came back from the first big office
on the circuit: “ When do the blank blanks arrive ?
Is Tom Couchman one of them?” Couchman
instantly went to the instrument and keyed tbe
reply: ‘‘ The blank blanks are here now,” and
gave his name as transmitted, “ Tom Couchman.”


Judging from recent happenings in the
Australian Supreme Courts, it really seems possible
that in time fraudulent lawyers may attain to the
dignity of getting a fair trial like other suspected
criminals.
History eternally repeats itself. Parnell,
who led in the British Commons the Irish Home
Rule cause—pre-eminently identified with Irish
Romanism—almost into sight of victory, was a
Protestant. Laurier, the present Canadian Prime
Minister, who has just crushed the Canadian
French-Romanist education movement, is a
Canadian French-Romanist himself ! Two telling
illustrations of the lost grip of sacerdotalism on the
politics of adult Democracy.

One Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It had been a bleai July day at our selec-
tion, and as night came on a bitter westerly
howled through the trees at Shingle Hut. Gold !
wasn’t it cold ! The pigs in the sty, hungry and
half-fed (we wanted for ourselves the few pump-
kins that had survived the drought) fought
savagely with each other for shelter, and squealed
all the time like —well, like pigs. The cows and
calves left the place to seek shelter away in the
mountains ; while the draught horses, their hair
standing up like barbed-wire, leaned sadly over
the fence and gazed at the green lucerne. Joe
went about shivering in an old coat of Dad’s with
only one sleeve to it —a calf had fancied the other
one day that Dad hung it on a post as a mark to
go by while ploughing.
“ My! it’ll be a stinger to-night,” Dad remarked
to Mrs. Brown, who sat, cold-looking, on the sofa,
as he staggered inside with an immense log for the
fire. A log ! Nearer a whole tree ! But wood
was nothing in Dad’s eyes.
Mrs. Brown had been at our place five or six
days. Old Brown called occasionally to see her,
so we knew they couldn’t have quarrelled. Some-
times she did a little housework, but more often
she didn’t. We talked it over together, but
couldn’t make it out. Joe asked mother, but she
had no idea—so she said. We were full up, as
Dave put it, of Mrs. Brown, and wished her out
of the place. She had taken to ordering us about,
as though she had something to do with us.
* * * *
After supper we sat round the fire—as
near to it as we could without burning ourselves —
Mrs Brown and all, and listened to the wind
whistling outside. Ah, it was pleasant beside the
fire listening to it! When Dad had warmed him-
self back and front he turned to us and said :
“ Now, boys, we must go directly and light some
fires and keep those wallabies back.”
That was a shock to us, and we looked at him
to see if he were really in earnest. He was, and
as serious as a judge.
“ To-night ? ” Dave answered, surprisedly—-
“ why to-night any more than last night or the
night before ? Thought you had decided to let
them rip ? ”
“Yes, but we might as well keep them off a bit
longer.”
“But there’s no wheat there for them to get now.
So what’s the good of watching them ? There’s no
sense in that.'”
Dad was immovable.
“ Any way ” —Joe chimed in—“ I'm not going—
not a night like this—not when I ain’t got any
boots.”
That vexed Dad. “ Hold your tongue, sir,”
he said—“you’ll do as you’re told.”
But Dave hadn’t finished. “ I’ve been follow-
ing that harrow since sunrise this morning,” he
said, “ and now you want me to go chasing walla-
bies about in the dark, a night such as this, and
for nothing else but to keep them from eating the
ground. It’s always the way here—the more one
doss the more he’s wanted to do,” and he com-
menced to cry.
Mrs. Brown had something to say. She agreed
with Dad and thought we ought to go, as the
wheat might spring up again.
“ Pshah ! ” Dave blurted out between his sobs,
while we thought of telling her to shut her mouth.
* * * *
Slowly and reluctantly we left that roaring
fireside to accompany Dad that bitter night. It
was a night !—dark as pitch, silent, forlorn and
forbidding, and colder than the busiest morgue.
And just to keep wallabies from eating nothing!
They had eaten all the wheat—every blade of it—
and the grass as well. What they would start on
next—ourselves or the cart-harness—wasn’t quite
clear. The vermin, truly, were innumerable and
had well-nigh taken possession of the selection.
We stumbled along in the dark one behind the
other, with our hands stuffed into our trousers.
Dad was in the lead, and poor Joe, bare shinned
and bootless, in the rear. Now and again
he tramped on a Bathurst-burr, and, in sitting
down to extract the prickle, would receive a
cluster of them elsewhere. When he escaped the
burr it was only to knock his shin against a log or
leave a toe-nail or two clinging to a stone. Joe
howled, but the wind howled louder and blew and
blew.
Dave, in pausing to wait on Joe, would mutter :
“ To hell with everything ! Whatever he wants
bringing us out a night like this, I’m damned if I
know ! ”
Dad couldn’t see very well in the dark, and on
this night couldn’t see at all, so he walked up
against one of the old draught horses that had
fallen asleep gazing at the lucerne. And what a
fright they both got! The old horse took it
worse than Dad—who only tumbled down—for he
plunged as though the devil had grabbed him, and
fell over the fence twisting every leg he had in the
wires. How the brute struggled ! We stood and
listened to him. After kicking panels of the
fence down and smashing every wire in it, he got
loose and made off, taking most of it with him.
“That’s one wallaby on the wheat, anyway,”
Dave remarked, and we giggled. We understood
Dave ; but Dad didn’t open his mouth.
We lost no time lighting the fires. Then we
walked through the “wheat” and wallabies!
May Satan reprove me if I exaggerate their num-
ber by one solitary pair of ears —from the row and
scatter they made there was a million.
* * * *
Dad told Joe, at last, he could go to sleep if
he liked, at the fire. Joe went to sleep — how, I don’t
know. Then Dad sat beside him, and for long in-
tervals would stare silently into the darkness.
Sometimes a string of the vermin would hop past
close to the fire, and another time a curlew would
come near and screech its ghostly wail, but he
never noticed them. Yet he seemed to be listen-
ing.
We mooched around from fire to fire, hour after
hour, and when we wearied of heaving fire-
sticks at the enemy we sat on our heels and cursed
the wind, and the winter, and the night-birds
alternately. , It was a lonely, wretched occu-
pation.
Now and again Dad would leave his fire to ask
us if we could hear a noise. We couldn’t, except
that of wallabies and rnopokes. Then he would go
back and listen again. He was restless, and,
somehow, his heart wasn’t in the wallabies at all.
Dave couldn’t make him out.
The night wore on. By-and-by there was a
sharp rattle of wires, then a rustling noise, and
Sal appeared in the glare of the fire. “ Dad ! ” she
said. That was all. Without a word, Dad bounced
up and went back to the house along with her.
“Something’s up!” Dave said, and, half-anxious,
half-afraid, we gazed into the fire and thought and
thought. Then we stared, nervously, into the
night, and listened for Dad’s return, but heard
only the wind and the mopoke.
At dawn he appeared again, with a broad smile
on his face, and told us that mother had got an-
other baby—-a fine little chap. Then we knew why
Mrs. Brown had been staying at our place.
Q.
STBELE RUDD.
Where the Flinders "( Flows.”
Respectfully Dedicated to “ P. Luftig.”
I have lately left the region
Where the Flinders “ flows ” ;
True —I found the flies were legion
Where the Flinders “ flows ” ;
But the thing that drove me frantic
Was your perjured poet’s antic
When he wrote that “ lie gigantic ”
That the Flinders “ flows.”
There are miles of arid distance
Where the Flinders “flows” ;
There are crows that feed on Christians
Where the Flinders “flows” ;
But no waters splash or splutter
In the desolation utter
Of that God-forsaken gutter
Where the Flinders “flows.”
Now, I’ve dogged that valley dreary
All in vain, God knows;
And I’ve sought until I’m weary
For the stream that “flows,”
In return for which infliction
May there light a malediction
On the bard who framed the fiction
That the Flinders “ flows.”

FLINDERS CROW.
Instead of Nansen for the Antarctic next
year and Borchgrevink the year after, why not
Nansen-cMm-Borehgrevink in one year ? The
eight millions of square miles scientists say lie
around the South magnetic Pole make an empire
big enough to accommodate a pair of Caesars.
In Launceston (Tas.) there is a District
Nursing Association, formed some years ago on an
unsectarian basis to provide competent nurses for
the poor at their own homes. A nurse has been
talking religion to her patients, and the thousand-
headed dragon of sectarianism has been roused
and sooled on to the angel in black and white with
all the vigor of olden time. The Protestant
churches are fighting each other over the affair,
some upholding and others opposing the action of
the Association in discharging the nurse. Result:
a new (presumably Protestant) association to be
formed forthwith.
Madden and Austin, lawyer and squatter,
and both freedom-of-contract men, are fathering a
bill in Vic. Assembly for the purpose of protecting
purchasers from the devices of sellers of artificial
manures. Madden was recently successful in
passing a similar bill dealing with the adulteration
of seeds. Both bills involve a serious interference
with the sacred principle of freedom of contract,
and the equally sacred shopkeepers’ palladium,
“ caveat emptor.” If the Legislature is in a mood
to pass at the instance of high-toned conserva-
tives adulteration-bills with respect to matters
mainly affecting boss-cockies and squatters, surely
the psychological moment has arrived for a general
Adulteration Act, abolishing the doctrine of cavca
emptor and protecting the helpless poor against
the poison and disease generated by the wholesale
adulteration tolerated by the wretched ethics of
our commercial life.
The Chow Prospectors .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the side of the hill facing the old water-logged
yellow flat, a fifteen-year-old field, now deserted
and solitary, I came unexpectedly upon a fe\V
Chinamen engaged in poking little holes in the
soft vegetable soil with long augers. Have you
seen workmen prospecting the streets for a gas-
leakage ? They drive an iron rod into the ground,
withdraw it, and an expert smells the hole. The
game is continued until the escape is duly located.
My Chinamen were acting similarly, excepting
that after each hole they critically examined the
end of the auger, and now and again a Mongolian
connoisseur smelt that with a studious and ab-
stracted air suggestive of an old wine-bibber trying
to ! * place ” a particular sample of port.
I was interested, and seated myself on a stump
to watch progress, and for some time the Chows
continued to probe the hill within a space of about
half-an-acre. They were extremely diligent and
deeply absorbed, boring and fingering and smell-
ing, and gave me no attention. But presently my
curiosity became unbearable. .
“ What you look him for, John ? ” I enquired of
a portly heathen who seemed to be the leader.
“Huh? No savee !” he replied, and continued
his work.
It struck me as an altogether novel kind of pro-
specting, and I ventured on the off-chance :
“No gold here, John. No wash. All down on
the flat, John.”
He did not favor me with even a glance, and the
work continued for half an hour, when I ventured
another question, addressing a small withered
subordinate Chow.
“ What you dig for, Paddy? You hide some-
thing here sometime ? ”
Visions of buried treasures had come to me
during the last ten minutes.
“No feeah,” replied the heathen. “Belly him
Poo Wong here longa tima go. You know where,
huh?”
They were boring for the remains of a long-dead
countryman.
I left, but heard later that they bottomed on
bones a little further up the hill.
